A LARGE BONUS.
The Company has recently forwarded to the holder of policy No. 278,127 a cheque for
£1,099 13s. 6d. in payment of the cash value of the Bonus for 1891, the
Policy being for 10,000, and issued in 1886, on the five year distribution plan. This
return is equal to an annual cash bonus of £2 4s. for each £lOO insured. Many of
these policies are reaching the bonus period with results very gratifying to the Insured. 1
The total payments to Policy-holders to December, 1890, amounted to £63,469,822,
of which upwards of £117,400,000 were bonus payments,—more than twice the amount of
Bonuses paid by any other Company. ■ ’

SATURDAY, APRIL 2, 1892.
Medical Fossildom.
New Socth Wales is, and for many years past
has been, the favourite hunting-ground of the
bogus or quack doctor. Owing to the astonish-
ing looseness of the law nearly any one may call
himself “Doctor” in that unhappy province,
and by virtue of a brass-plate and a huge red
lamp may proceed to try strange and gruesome
experiments on the human inside with com-
parative impunity. The mysterious hangers-on
of the medical profession are practically in-
numerable. The nervous-debility man, the
“lost manhood” practitioner, the individual
who preys on the citizen who doesn’t feci well,
the faith-healer, the mesmerist, the electro-
magnetic person, the individual who wants to
have £1 forwarded in stamps, the one who
wants 10s. in stamps, the liver-and-kidney
artist, the lost-vitality genius, the clairvoyant ;
the double-barrelled or combination trance-
medium who cures all diseases, and foretells the
future, and locates lost dogs, and gives legal
advice all in one act, and a hundred other sorts,
descriptions, and varieties of assorted physicians
are on the loose from one end of the province to
the other. Some of them are qualified ; the
vast majority are not. Most of their modes of
treatment are just about as effective as
if they stirred up the vitals of their
patients with a long, iron-shod pole. A
very large percentage are hideously illiterate
and live by the simple but efficient
process of scaring large sums of money out of
feeble-minded invalids, and then throwing them
out in a half-dead condition when their funds
are exhausted. They give strange death-certi-
ficates, and do curious things in matters relating,
to illegitimate infants. Therefore there has
been, for many years past, a loud and incessant
demand for an efficient Medical Bill which will
block the bogus physician in his devastating
track, and, after much waiting, a measure to
effect this purpose has at last come before the
Legislature. That is to say, the new Medical
Bill has been drawn ostensibly with a view to
the abolition of quackery. But, unhappily,
the drafting of it seems to have been left to the
most bigoted, narrow-minded, unprogressive,
and hopelessly intolerant members of a narrow-
minded and intolerant profession, and it is so
devised that it merely gratifies the private
grudges and animosities of the “legitimate”
medical fraternity, and leaves the quack very
much where he was.
* * *
In the first place, the Medical Bill does not,
in any sense, prevent the bogus doctor from
practising. The utter and irredeemable
quack—the one who has no degree whatsoever
—will be required to intimate on his doorplate
or in his advertisements that he is “not
registered.” Some very successful members of
the tribe, however, do not even pretend to be
registered, and to them the clairvoyant,
the trance-idiot, and their kind—the mea-
sure will make no difference. The “ legiti-
mate ” physician has no serious objection
to these people. They lie out of his sphere and
inflict no serious injury on his dignity. The
individual whom he regards with utter loathing
is the qualified doctor who treads on that un-
unwritten code which is described as the
“etiquette of the profession,” and the Medical
Bill is framed principally for his annihilation.
It provides, in the first place, for the appoint-
ment of a Medical Council consisting of
ten practitioners, five to be appointed by
the University Senate, and live by the Gover-
nor. The University Senate is a notoriously
conservative, bigoted, and fossilised institution,
and the Governor, it may be safely presumed,
knows nothing of medical science whatsoever.
This Council of Ten will rule the medical
register of New South Wales ; and as every un-
registered practitioner will be required to pro-
claim himself as a quack on his doorplate and
his professional card, the Council’s power of
getting level with its personal enemies is prac-
tically unlimited. It can do comparatively
little to the absolute and acknowledged swindler
—he will get on in Ids avowedly unregistered
state by peddling loathsome patent nostrums,
by exploiting nervous patients, by performing
illegal operations, and by blackmail, but when it
disqualifies a genuine practitioner —one who will
not live by such devices—for some insignificant
breach of medical etiquette, it virtually con-,
signs him to starvation.
# * *
A large number of different bodies exercise
the power of conferring degrees, and the holder
of a degree, under the proposed system, must
register himself with the Medical Council of
New South Wales in order that he may be
qualified to practise within the province. If
the body which conferred the degree sees fit to
strike the holder thereof from its register the
Medical Council will straightway withdraw his
local registration, and thus reduce him to the
status of a quack. The original degree was
conferred because the recipient was learned in
the art of medicine ; when it is cancelled he
remains equally learned and, in almost every
case, equally trustworthy, but he is obliterated
as a physician merely because he has done
something contrary to the “ etiquette ” of the
profession. Drunkenness and immorality are not
necessarily contrary to professional etiquette,
but it is fatal to advertise in the press or by cir-
cular. Also, it is a deadly sin to believe in or
practise homoeopathy, hydropathy or mesmerism
—or to meet in consultation any practitioner wdio
practises any of these forbidden arts, or to use
or recommend any patent, proprietary, or secret
medicine. When any doctor transgresses any
of these unwritten laws, and the body by which
he was originally registered hears of the fact
and cancels his registration, then the Medical
Council will straightway blast him for ever-
more, so far as its jurisdiction extends. When
any doctor so transgresses, it may be safely
assumed that the body by which he is registered
will hear of it—the Medical Council, without
the slightest doubt, will provide for that. But,
as it happens, the Council can only act after the
offender s degree has been cancelled, and here a
curious discrepancy comes in. Such a body as
the Royal College of Surgeons is intensely zealous
of the etiquette of the profession, and the person
who ranks as a member or even as a fellow
of that honourable body will be promptly
blighted if he outrages the combustible feelings
of the Council, and leads them to report him
for lowering the dignity of his art. On the
other hand, the man who merely owns a licence
from the Apothecaries’ Society is proof against
almost all chances. He holds the meanest,
cheapest, and least important degree which a
practitioner can hold, and yet be permitted to
fool with the human frame—a degree which
only declares him to be one remove higher than
the person who pounds drugs or turns the handle
of a pill-machine in a chemist’s shop. But the
Apothecaries’Society is so low-down that it pays
no heed to medical etiquette; it is the last refuge
of the medical outcast, and has no dignity to
lose, and, therefore, it seldom takes cognisance
of anything short of wilful murder. Conse-
quently, when two physicians commit some
paltry deviation from the cast-iron rules of the
trade the man of great learning and the highest
skill will be promptly cast out, and the one who
is only one remove from an ignoramus will con-
tinue to be a qualified doctor to the end. Under
pretence of saving the public from quacks the
man of the highest standing and ability will be
made a quack by Act of Parliament, and the
man who was next door to a quack all along will
go on as before.
* * #
That item in the unwritten code of the pro-
fession which forbids the use of any “secret
remedy ” is another fair sample of the incurable
conservatism of the profession. The average
“secret remedy”—the remedy which the in-
ventor deliberately keeps secret long after it
has passed the experimental stage—ds, it must
be admitted, .generally a delusion, and the
owner of it, an utterly soulless money-grubber.
Also the ordinary patent medicine is a thing
of which the less that is said the better. It is
reasonable, in the interests of the public, to
prevent any physician, under the most severe
penalties, from peddling his own or other people’s
patent remedies to his patients. But the clause
in question goes much further than this. Koch’s
consumption cure was, for many months, a
“ secret remedy,” and Koch himself could have
been disqualified under this far-reaching item
of medical etiquette. Pasteur’s discovery—or
alleged discovery—of a remedy for hydrophobia
comes under the same category. Every new
medicine must necessarily be more or less of a
“secret” in its early stages in order that
the discoverer may bring it to perfection—
or to something approaching|perfection—other-
wise he must turn it loose in its crude state in
order that dull or ignorant practitioners may
misuse it as a means for the wholesale destruc-
tion of their patients. Therefore, the Medical
Bill, in this aspect, becomes a means whereby
the skilled or enterprising physician who dis-
covers something new in the domain of medical
science may be promptly disqualified, banned,
and ruined by those ancient and stupid persons
who never discover anything, should the fossils
feel so inclined. The chances are that the
power would not be used, but its existence is
an outrage none the less. It is a scheme by
which Morell Mackenzie— who, despite his
vanity and his self-glorification, was still head
and shoulders above the ruck of his tribe—
might bo officially abolished, while the
drunken back-blocks sawbones, whose ideas
never, by any chance, soar above calomel and
aloes, would go on his dismal way with honour
to the end of his days. Morell Mackenzie,
in fact, was removed from the roll of the
College of Surgeons in his later days, for
quitting the ancient groves of his profession ;
this fact made little difference in England, but
in New South Wales it would, under the
legislation now proposed, reduce him to the
level of the clairvoyant who undertakes, in the
columns of the evening press, to diagnose all
complaints in a trance for 2s. Gd.
* #
The one thing which the public desires is
that, when it goes in search of a qualified prac-
titioner or an unqualified practitioner, it
should be able to get the article it is looking
for without any possibility of deception.
The Medical Bill undertakes, distinctly and in
so many words, to fill this want, and in so
doing it utters a glaring and wilful lie. A
qualified physician, so far as the public are
concerned, is merely a man who has
acquired a certain amount of medical know-
ledge and who can produce satisfactory evidence
from some body fitted to judge of his capabili-
ties, that he possesses such knowledge. So
long as this one point is settled beyond dispute,
people care not one solitary straw whether
he advertises or does not advertise, whether he
practises homeeopathy, hydropathy, or mes-
merism in addition to his other pursuits,
whether he consults with homoeopathists
or hydropathists, whether he believes in
Mattei’s cancer cure or Koch’s consump-
tion specific or any other secret or non-
secret remedy or device; and when any
Medical Council asserts that a qualified
doctor, because he treads on these old profes-
sional fads, is unqualified, it thereby utters a
malignant falsehood. Homoeopathy was the
first beginning of a great and beneficent revolu-
tion in medical science ; it wiped out the old
school of Thugs who bled and blistered their
patients and filled them up with oceans of
nefarious drugs; and the spectacle of ten
nominees claiming authority to practically
abolish homoeopathy in New South Wales is an
act of ignorant intolerance and one worthy
of the old days of the Inquisition. Hydro-
pathy has performed so many undoubted
cures that the demand put forward by
the mouldy pill-mongers- of the Legislative
Council for authority to put their official ban
upon this branch of medical science is as
grotesque as the demand of a worm for permis-
sion to put an extinguisher on a star. The one
point about the Medical Bill which admits of
no possible doubt is that it is not intended in
any sense as a protection for the XRiblic, but
solely to make the medical profession a close
corporation for the exclusive benefit of its own
members. As a conspicuous example of this
any proviso that a physician or a suraeon might
be disqualified for drunkenness, and particu-
larly for drunkenness while in attendance on a
patient, has been cheerfully expunged. A
surgeon, therefore, may be habitually drunk
while on duty, and thereby mangle and
hideously vivisect his clients with impunity—
they are only the general public, and
the general public, except as a means
of obtaining fees, doesn’t matter. But the
surgeon who is strictly sober and highly
qualified and who puts an advertisement in a
newspaper shall be straightway abolished and
falsely declared to be a quack, for the adver-
tisement is contrary to the “ dignity of the pro-
fession,” and the Medical Council is to be
created to uphold that and to let all other
things slide.
Black Queensland.
Whilst Queensland under Samuel Griffith
is preparing to re-embark in the Kanaka slave-
trade, a few facts gleaned from her own blood-
stained records may arrest the attention and
arouse the conscience of white Australia. The
ships Ceara, Lizzie, Hopeful, Forest King,
Heath and Sybil were busy during the year
1884 procuring cheap and reliable labour for
the Queensland sugar plantations. In slave-
trade slang, they were “ blackballing. ” Each
ship flew the British flag and carried an
agent accredited by the Queensland Govern-
ment. By this we know that they were
not engaged in the slave-trade proper, be-
cause slavery cannot exist under the British flag.
Just about this period, for some reason of its
own, the Queensland Government was on “the
virtuous lay,” and a horrible suspicion entered
its mind—and was duly advertised to a vir-
tuous world—that there was a bare possibility
that a savage of the South Sea Islands, who
had no idea beyond sleeping “ with his
head in the shade and his feet in the
sun,” might not correctly understand the
exact legal meaning of the agreement which
he was supposed to have entered into.
Exeter Hall having been duly advised, the
Queensland Government started to work off the
attack of virtue by the appointment of a Royal
Commission “ to enquire into the circumstances
under which labourers have been introduced
into Queensland from New Guinea and other
Islands,” etc. The Commission enquired and
reported that every man of them—62s—had
been kidnapped ; and they incidentally found
out other things which will be set forth more or
less briefly in this article. The people of Queens-
land were horrified at the disclosures ; the poor
kidnapped wretches—or so many of them as
had not already escaped through the portals of
death—were sent back with a certain amount
of theatrical display to their islands; and the
people of Queensland solemnly, by Act of
Parliament, declared that on 31st December,
1890, the hateful traffic should absolutely come
to an end for ever.
• # #
This Royal Commission consisted of three
members : —Mr. F. J. Buukland, M.L.A. for
Bulimba—the seat for which two candidates
(one white, one black) are now scram-
bling; W. Kinnaird Rose, and Mr. Mil-
man, now Stamp Commissioner. They were
all three the abject worshippers of Griffith,
and he has ever since accepted, or affected to
accept, all their statements as gospel. The trio
started on their travels and examined the 1
brought to Queensland by those six slm, , s
lather those of them who were left alive •TT 1 '
lime. The labours of the Commission' 1 *" l(i
somewhat lightened by the little circunwi"^ 0
that before they got to the plantations , a * Vce
154 per cent, of the “ cheap and °
lost all chance of ever seeing their is'an.l 1 ' ll(
again. They were dead. The total im!T*
brought had been 025. Of these 97 j ui 1 '° l>
beyond their troubles. Eight voyages' j fc
had been made by these ships, the Ceari'- 1

The Commissioners expressed their opinion
as to the methods of recruiting employed durin»
these eight voyages as follows : 0
No. I.—Our opinion is that all the recruits brought h v
the Ceara on this voyage wore seduced aboard on
falsa pretences; that the nature of their eima-vnwiits
were never fully explained to them ; that they” had little
or no comprehension of the kind of work t hey had to
perform ; and that the period for which they agreed to
come was in no single instance three years. “
No. 2.-We are of opinion that not one of the labourers
brought by the Lizzie on this voyage agreed when
recruited, to serve and remain in Queensland for three
years; that the nature of their engagements was never
clearly explained to or understood by them, and that the
method of recruiting was cruelly deceptive and altogether
illegal. 0
No. 3.—Our opinion is that a system of deliberate fraud
was practised iu engaging all the recruits duriiw the
voyage; that their engagements were not explained to
them in any sense approximately correct; and that none
of them believed they had agreed to remain and serve in
Queensland for three years.
No. 4. On a review of the wliole evidence as to re-
cruiting on this voyage of tire Lizzie, we are of opinion
that, while some of the natives were forcibly kidnapped
all of them were allured 011 board by false statements’
that the nature of the engagements to which they sub-
sequently attached their marks was deliberately mis-
represented to them ; and that they had no clear under-
standing they were coming to Queensland to work on a
sugar plantation for three years.
No. 5. We are of opinion that none of the recruits on
board the Hopeful were lawfully recruited, and that not
one understood ho had engaged to remain and serve in
the colony for three years.
No. C.—We are of opinion that the attempts made to
explain the nature of the engagements to the recruits,
both at their islands and at the port of arrival, were
wholly inadequate ; that the engagements were not fully
understood by the recruits, and that none of them appre-
ciated that the term of service for which they had left
their homos was three years.
No. 7.—We are of opinion that all the recruits brought
by the Forest King were decoyed on board under false
pretences; that the nature of their agreement was nevi r
explained to them; and that none of them understoid
they were to work on a sugar plantation for any period,
much less for three years.
No. 8. —Our opinion is that recruits brought by the
Heath were enticed on board under false pretences; that
the nature of their engagements was never satisfactorily
explained to them ; and that none of them comprehended
they were coming to Queensland tc wc<k on a sugar
plantation for three years.
* * #
“Iu order to secure labourers,” the report
says, “the principal device of the recruiting-
agent would seem to have been an appeal to the
cupidity of the savage. He displayed a tempt-
ing array of tomahawks, knives, tobacco, and
calico—in fact, those articles most coveted by
the inhabitants of the islands of the southern
seas The love of home of these
islanders amounts to a passion, and the recruit-
ing-agents had to overcome dislike to practical
exile by assurances that they would not be
absent for any length of time. . . . Even
under the most favourable circumstances the
natives had very little conception of the real
purpose for which they were invited on board
or engaged to go on the trip to Queensland.”
F6or creatures —children of an enfeebled, dying
race—what else could they do when trapped by
Christian cunning in the service of Christian
greed ? When the poor, frightened wretches on
board the Ceara found themselves “in deep
water ” they began to talk among themselves
and cried. The boys on the Lizzie, on hearing
that they had to work on sugar-plantations for
an extended period, wept bitterly. In the
hands of their Christian taskmaster they felt
themselves as helpless as the rabbit in the folds of
the anaconda. “ They seemed,” says the report,
“ to have accepted whatever the ‘ big Govern-
ment master ’ said as the inevitable, and their
only resource was to go below and cry.” In
too many instances it was the cry of the broken
heart never to be healed.

Is Honesty the Best Policy?
Editor Bulletin, —For about twelve months
flow the Victorian papers have, Aveek by Aveek,
chronicled fresh eases of fraud and dishonesty,
and after following these cases up through the
Police Court, the Sessions, the receiving gaol,
aud finally the country gaol or the penal estab-
lishment at Pentridge, avc are compelled to
believe that, to the offender of a certain species,
honesty is the biggest kind of folly. I have
investigated a number of cases, and find that,
111 nine out of ten, providing the offender goes
about the affair in a cool, calm, business-like
fashion, the proceeding pays a satisfactory
dividend—as a matter of policy it is eminently
successful.
• * *
There are a fcA» essentials to a successful
fraud. Firstly, it is desirable, though not
Recessary, that the offender shall hold a good
position, both commercially and socially ; if he
he a member in good standing of some church
so much the better. Secondly, the amount of
his “ haul ” must be in no ease under £2OOO,
and that is cutting it very fine ; if he can
run it up to £200,000 so much the bolter.
Thirdly (this is only in case he is arrested), he
must have one or two influential friends who
must be able to draAV upon the proceeds of his
“ haul.” With these three postulates it is
possible iu Victoria to-day to commit a fraud
of almost any magnitude, and to escape practi-
cally without punishment and Avith the pro-
ceeds of the said fraud. Even in the event of
the big offender ultimately coming down with a
sickening thud, he gains, all the way through,
an amount of consideration that is never
extended to the uneducated and“irrespectable”
criminal. By A'arious indirect means it is
possible to get one’s trial postponed until it lias
a chance to come oft' before a lenient Judge ; it
is possible to interview and come to an under-
standing Avith jurymen; it may be possible to
call and see the Governor of the gaol where the
prisoner is confined, should he he unfortunate
enough to come to that pass ; it is possible to
interview Avarders with very satisfactory
results—both to Avarders and prisoner. The
medical adviser to the gaol Avill often be
found to be “amenable.” In fact, in one
word, the present judicial and penal system of
Victoria is a huge fraud. To the man who,
being friendless and poor, commits an offence,
it means generally lifelong imprisonment, perse-
cution and misery ; but to him who possesses
the essentials mentioned above, it may fairly be
described as a picnic, a good joke.
# # #
Here are a feAV instances. Butler, ex-
registrar of titles, defrauded the people of
Victoria of large sums, and was aAvarded seven
years’ “hard.” He is at present, and Avill
remain so, governor’s clerk at Pentridge ; he
dresses differently from other prisoners (no
knickers or stockings), has another prisoner
assigned to wait on him, to make up his bed —■
Avhich includes a mattress—clean his boots,
brush his clothes, etc.; he sleeps in a room, not
iu a cell, by himself, is addressed as “ Mister ”
Butler, and is, in fact, treated quite as a
gentleman should be. About the middle of
last year lavo prisoners arrived at Castlemaine
gaol. One had, as a clerk, embezzled £l3 from
his employers at Ballarat, and received fifteen
months, the stolen money having been returned
before the trial by his Avidowed mother; the
other Avas Edward Mirams, avlio received
twelve months for embezzlement. Upon bis
brother’s release, Edward Mirams petitioned the
Executive Council for a remission of his sentence,
which Avas promptly reduced to six months.
The clerk didn’t get any remission. He had no
influential brother—only a poor old mother,
Avho didn’t know her Avay round. I could give
many instances of Avell-connected or distin-
guished prisoners avlio pass the time quite
pleasantly. Charles Rivers Allpress holds
the position (one can hardly call it a situation)
of doctor’s assistant at Pentridge ; his hands
are never soiled by such a degrading thing as
Avork. Gentlemen avlio hold these responsible
positions, mind you, see the daily papers, the
latest novels, &c., smoke cigarettes, and I Avill
Avager Mr. Allpress is by this time quite a
connoisseur in the matter of Avines and spirits.
Their gas is not turned off at eight, nor have
they to sleep on the floor of the cell Avith an
allowance of four “ blankets ” in summer and
five in Avinter, as has Bill Smith, housebreaker,
or Tom Larrikin, pocket-picker.
* # *
These arc representative cases, not isolated
ones. To come doAvu to recent times. George
N. Taylor, Avho is generally considered to have
made away Avith nearly £lOO,OOO, some of
which he Avill, no doubt, be able to realise
Avhen he comes out, received two years hard
labour. He Avill bo out, so far as his pre-
sent sentence goes, about August 23, 1893.
He Avill probably go to Pentridge, Avhcre ho
Avill be kept in separate confinement about three
months, after Avhich he Avill probably be put to
some light clerical work. Last Aveek James
Richards was charged, at the City Court with
having no A’isihlc means of support. Constable
Brennan stated that he had seen him loafing
round and that he Avas a bad character generally,
and then Richards, avlio had enjoyed just a
month’s liberty after having served a two years’
sentence for housebreaking, Avas forthwith dis-
patched by Mr. Craib, J.P., to Pentridge for
another two years ; after the expiration of
Avhich he will probably bo dispatched for
another two years, and so on ad infinitum.
N. B. — Richards will not be out till March
’94, and don’t you forget it. This offender,
Avho is described by Mr, Craib as a “ most
dangerous man Ukljc at large,” will he sent to
the quarries—-after doing some time separately
—where he Avill possibly get an eye blown out
or an arm torn off, in Avhich case he Avill
doubtless be less dangerous than formerly.
There arc exceedingly ugly Avhispers going
round Melbourne in connection with Taylor’s
sentence. If there be the slightest ground for
these, then the province is indeed “ on her
knees.”
* * *
As regards the Victorian gaols, too much
poAver lies in the hands of the Governors
and the Visiting-Justice. Should prisoner
gain the favour of the former official, he spends
much less time in separate confinement, and
is subsequently much better treated than
one avlio has the misfortune to offend
him, though botli may be serving equal
sentences for similar offences. The Visit-
ing-Justice is too often unreasonable. (Very
mild !) Fancy adding 12 months to a young
man’s sentence for attempting suicide, as was
done at Pentridge not long ago ! Great God !
think of punishiny a man who wants, avlio
is determined, to take his oavu life! And
again a Avoman at Castlemaine was in
January this year sentenced by a Visit-
ing J.P. to three Avceks solitary confinement on
bread and water (no Avork except to count the
bricks in four bare Avails), and 12 hours in a
dark cell underground, for insulting and in-
decent language to a warder. It is satisfactory
to learn that the woman has not yet lost her
reason. This three Aveeks (as is all solitary) is
cumulative, and entails a three weeks extension
of the original sentence. By the Avay, most
persons would be very much astonished, if not
shocked, could they learn the number of men
avlio yearly pass through the observation and
padded cells of the various gaols, driven mad
by the isolation, Avhich is aAvful and terrible to
a man of Aveak brain or a nervous temperament.
* * *
To sum up, Avithout the three essentials men-
tioned before honesty undoubtedly is the best
of all possible “policies,” but given these three
it is easy to make fraud of all descriptions pay
a much better dividend than the depositors are
getting out of the “ krouk ” building societies
and land banks. And it is the business of the
people of Victoria to see that this anomaly is
remedied.—Yours, etc., J.B.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Bald-Headed Nation.
According to recent advices from England,
that much-advertised pearl-stealer, Mrs. Ethel
Osborne, now doing nine months in gaol for
perjury, is suffering from the complication of
complaints which invariably descends on the
well-connected person when in trouble. She
is afflicted with hysteria and fits ; also,
she is about to be confined; and further,
she refuses to take food, is in a comatose con-
dition, and is in danger of becoming insane, and
on top of all these diseases it is announced that
she is “ sinking.” Therefore, the usual efforts
are being made to procure her release, and it is
believed in many quarters that Home Secretary
Matthews —probably the most flinty-hearted
hangman, when any of the “ lower orders ” are
concerned, who has held office in England
for half-a-century—will remit her sentence. It
would seem that “ the great heart of England”
feels deep sympathy for Mrs. Osborne. Her
crime, it is true, was about the meanest and
most gratuitous thing in the way of perjury
that has turned up in recent times. She robbed
her friend in the first place, and, having done
this, she deliberately attempted to ruin the
lady she had injured in law-costs and damages,
and to brand her as a slanderer and her
husband as a ruined gambler and a thief.
Her case was worse than that of the
Ticiiborne Claimant, even with that gen-
tleman’s assertion that he had seduced his
cousin thrown in with his other iniquities, or
alleged iniquities, and yet he got fourteen years
instead of only nine months. But the Claimant
was middle-aged, corpulent, and not beautiful,
and therefore the country didn’t sympathise
with him to any extent worth mentioning, nor
does it sympathise with anyone of the hundred
other perjurers who are doing from three to
seven-year sentences for offences which, by
comparison with Mrs. Osborne’s, are trivial in
the extreme. It is the British nation that is
suffering from hysteria and epileptic fits over
this interesting prisoner—not the prisoner her-
self. The two really noteworthy features in
the case are the criminal’s unlucky husband
and her unborn child. The latter will be
haunted all through life by the recollection that
it was born within the stone walls of a gaol,
and no State should fasten such a stigma on
any citizen if it can reasonably be avoided. But
in this instance the infant seems to have been
deliberately used as an element to excite sym-
pathy. Mrs. Osborne could easily have lain
low in her safe refuge on the Continent until
after her confinement ; the British authorities
showed no desire to demand her extradition,
and the Spanish Government assuredly had no
idea of expelling her from its territories.
Apparently she came back when she did
simply that her condition might excite
the sentimentality of the nation, and arouse
the finer feelings of the Judge, and the
result has answered all expectations. As
to Captain Osborne, he has acted as
might be expected either from a gentleman
of the noblest character, or from an exceed-
ingly astute man of the world who knew
exactly how to make the best of a bad situation.
He, no doubt, took the course he did from the
highest motives, but, in any case, it was the
oxdy course which promised to pay a reasonable
dividend. The whole case is an evidence that
the Anglo-Saxon is getting washy in his old
age ; the attitude of England in general is not
that of a country which lias a deep and reliable
sense of justice, but rather that of an unctuous
old bald-head in the front row at a leg-show.
Mrs. Osborne is the leg-show, and the entire
nation is the bald-headed old party in the front
row, and it feels benevolent just as the old man
does when the ballet is in progress, and for
pretty much the same reason.
The following, which purports to be a message
from a converted New Guinea nigger to the
people of England, is published in the North
Queensland Herald —the still, small voice of
the poor heathen grovelling in a far-distant land
and offering his loyal cocoanut to Victoria : —
Our forefathers did not come here. What is the reason
that 1 am here ? Only one reason, God’s word : we now
travel and are at peace. Another reason is this: if we
•do wrong the Governor and his servants have taught us,
and we are now a little better. Our forefathers were not
wise, they were in the dark ; now the men of Britain have
come to live in our villages, we are glad and live at peace
with one another. Now you correct us for our misdeeds,
we are living peaceably and travel empty-handed. We
New Guineans are glad, our stomachs are good. Victoria
has taken possession of our country, it is very good, she
alone is powerful and strong.
It is a pitiable and abject thing when a hypo-
critical missionary sits down to solemnly concoct
tootsy-wootsy drivel like this and pass it off as
the effort of a massive savage two yards long,
and armed with a big spiked club. Wherever
the missionary goes there always arises some
horrid narrative of a huge, elderly cannibal in
a pinafore and a feeding-bottle, who sent a
slobbering baby-message to Queen Victoria ;
and it makes ns feel sick every time we come
across it.
♦
“ Savage ” writes us as follows : —
Dear B,—The flogging question, as carried on at
present, has two sides only. There is a Total Abstinent e
and a Yell Extortion party. Why not a Mediumistic
Faction? An ordinary hoy merely whimpers under in
ordinary caning. The “shrieking blasphemies” of the
triangksare no more known in schools than the “cat”
itself. How strange it is that the advocates of whipping
should never retreat to such far more tenable ground ?
You say the body of Europe (Russia being too barbaious
to count) has abolished the Rod in toto. I submit—
first, that you ignore the Boy Element. You say,
“No man,” etc. The male youth of Germany, etc.,
is legally hideahle or not. I don’t know. Bub
I suspect. And by thrashing the child you may,
possibly, reduce at once the amount and the gravities
of adult crime, and so be less impelled to what I will
admits if you like, to lie extremity. Secondly, an equiva-
lent may exist in severer discipline. Thirdly, certain
abominations (larrikinism) known to us are far less com-
mon there, and again there is less occasion for severity.
The subject is like that of suicide. The firmest
opponents of self-destruction must admit exceptions to
the rule. Or, like that of the capital penalty, which is
really unarguable in the face of European armaments.
So long as a million of legally innocent men can bo
put to mutual slaughter over practically nothing,
it seems slightly inconsistent to attack the execution
of a few hundreds convicted of foul murder. When you
say “At present gaol is a farce,” you strengthen the
position of the oorporalists. This, too, is loose. Darling-
hurst, no doubt, contains those who have hardly known
as good meals outside, certainly not as good beds, or the
luxury of being free from vermin. Some of them might
he glad of such winter quarters, even with a couple of
“ dozen ” attached. In their case, no doubt, the humor-
ous element enters. But it is not so with the stockman,
who was tempted by the rich man’s racer. And not so
with the bank-director. And when is the prison system
to ho altered? When is the age of moral suasion
to the law-breaker to commence? Where are the
signs? Not very visible in a country where the vice of
gambling has increased eighty-fold in eight yeais
(if the figures quoted in New South Wales Assembly by
Mr. Lamb are correct). Then, in default of a rational
mode of confinement, why not try a plan founded on that
humane idea of child-government, which limits pain to
the hounds of self-control? Then, as to “ degradation.”
By British law boys can be birched up to sixteen years.
But will anyone say that a patient of a week past sixteen
is bound to he morally injured? The distinction must he
arbitary. Some infants are more sensitive this way than
their fathers. And is there no shame in being shaven
and shorn, and sent to bed at sunset ? Bah! Boys don’t
feel shy at being “licked,” but at having had to “holler.”
I was once in charge of some young hoys, and, of course,
had to punish somehow. For bullying bis junior (after
being warned), I gave a 10-year-old his choice between a
specified “ spanking ” and bed straight after supper. lie
elected the flesh ! I thought awhile, and became an
adherent of the short and simple in strict moderation.

The chronic or incurable preacher is the same
in all ages and under all circumstances. The
other day, in the columns of the London Times,
not three but forty preachers accumulated
themselves, and published over their signatures
a joint and several document entitled a
“Declaration of the Truth of the Holy Scrip-
ture.” There is something grotesque in the
idea of forty preachers writing to a newspaper
to guarantee the Lord, but evidently none of
them noticed the fact; the ordinary curate is a
dreadfully humourless animal. They didn’t
advance any reason for their statements, but
merely announced in a flat-footed, bald-headed
fashion that the Scriptures were true, and
signed their own ignoble cognomens of Smith
and Jones and the like, as the sole proof of
their assertion. They didn’t state how they
knew the facts of the case better than anyone
else, neither did they call any evidence to prove
their reliability. It evidently didn’t occur to
them that if the Scripture couldn’t stand with-
out their support, it wasn’t likely to stand any
better with it, or that the creed which has
lasted for nineteen centuries -was much less in
Avant of outside assistance than the parson who
only lasts for 40 years or thereabouts The
Avhole position was very much like forty worms
leaving a signed document behind them when
they died, setting forth that they had inspected
the Alps, and were prepared to guarantee them
as a reliable range of mountains and as being
all there. And having flopped down this heavy
declaration the forty proceeded to state, on their
own authority, that no one should question or
inquire into the truth of anything in the Bible
save in a proper spirit—in other words, when
anybody met Avith any statement in that volume
which he wouldn’t believe if he met it in any
other A'olume, he shouldn’t inquire into it unless
he had first made up his mind that he wouldn’t
believe anything Avhich he might discover in the
course of the inquiry. Then lhey wound up by
announcing that they—the forty preachers in
question —didn’t object to anybody using his
reasoning powers to cast light upon any state-
ments which he found in the Bible provided he
first accepted the absolute “ genuineness and
authenticity” of the “living oracles,” and
merely inquired into the meaning of their
Avords—if he felt any doubts about the words
themselves or the Avritcrs of them they—the
preachers aforesaid—Avould evidently fall on
him Avith a thud. And now it is to he hoped
that the Avhole business is settled. W hen 40
expounders Avrite to a paper to state that every-
thing is all right, from the beginning of all things
up to now, and from hoav till the end of
eternity, then the entire business ought to be
set tled for evermore.

his defalcations made a “ miscarriage of justice”
impossible, hut Melbourne people will not soon

have been ready to make good a £lO,OOO deficit,
anyAvay. So long as his robberies were sup-
posed to be limited to that trifle there was an
active conspiracy (warmly approved by a section
of the press) to whitewash clear old Matt, and
keep that respectable sinner out of gaol. Up
to £15,000, perhaps, the felony Avould have
found Avilling compounders. Which is Avorth
considering in connection Avith the circumstance
that one Hastings, an English M.P., has, in the
ordinary course of things, gone to gaol for
seven years for embezzling that same amount of
trust-money. The reader is at liberty to put
tAvo and two together, and deduce his own
conclusions.

The largely feminine committee of the Doll-and-Pln-
cushion contest, in aid of the Carington Convalescent
Hospital, announce, as a thrilling and welcome novelty in
connection with their show, that “a special room will be
provided where parcels can be left! ” Really some people
don’t know enough of human nature to be trusted out
alone! I would like to see people availing themselves of
the precious “room,” wouldn’t you? Those who pur-
chase at bazaars don’t generally want to keep their
expenditure a deep, dark secret, and rather than abandon
one parcel they would go from stall to stall laden like
packhorses. It is, in fact, de rigucur at fancy fairs, o
w hatever you call them, to go about 'a len down with odds
and ends, and I have seen a well-known Judge going down
the middle of the show with three feather-dusters and a
fire-screen in his hand which he wouldn’t have re-
linquished for anything. Indeed, so great is the mania to
appear as if breaking down under the burden of your
v isihle charities that people of large pretensions and
small means have been known to bring parcels in tin' r
pockets and then flourish them, when they' got inside, as
part of the day’s philanthropic expenditure! A room
to leave parcels in, forsooth ! I must break off here to
smile.
It is rather questionable policy, on Lady Blank’s port,
to visit the sins of the few on (he many—it is playing
ducks and drakes with those silken bonds, this sudden

Because, you see, the “ loyal ” section of Australian

dear old mother country wants in Australia to find out
that when their Excellencies fail in the only State duty
left them-(which is to be bell-wether for the “classes”
in their moments of relaxation)-there is no perceptible
falling-off in the fun—and if Lady Blank isn’t careful
there will be no telegraphed, “ Well done, thou good and
faithful servant,” from Windsor Castle when she goes
home again.
Which reminds of the funny little habit the same
Gracious Personage has of only looking an inch beyond
her nose whenever she lucklessly finds herself in public.
I suppose she does it so as really not to see unpleasant
people who are bobbing right and left, hut it gives the
august lady a complicated expression —something between
short-sight and a squint-and the effect isn’t worth win-
ning. It would be far less wear-and-tear to her facial
features, if the lady picked out the least objectionable
people and returned their salutes— but if she prefers not.
to, it really is nobody’s business, is it? For my part, I
am always vastly amused at the finished discomfiture of
the smartly-dressed woman who aims a reverent inclina-
tion at those unseeing eyes—and then wilts visibly.

Monsieur D’Arc’s Fantoccini on view at Sydney Royal
Standard Theatre (gotten up with blue and crimson
draperies, pot plants, and mirrors to a wonderful degree
of smartness, by the way) arc certainly the most gifted
marionettes Sidney has ever thumped its approving
palms at, and their dressing and staging are tco dazzling
for words. Especially in the burlesque of “ Bluebeard ”
this is the time for Bluebeard, by the way—jewels and
tinsel and silks and satins are as plentiful as leaves on a
tree, and the transformation-scenes, which follow a
decidedly funny harlequinade, are genuinely “corusca-
ting,” and all that sort of thing. Above all, there is a
distinct flavour of Paris about the clever show and the
singing—jingles set to popular airs—is very good of its
kind. Every parent who has a deserving little Johnny
hungering and thirsting for an outing is sure to infest the
Fantoccini.
“ New Men and Old Acres,” at Criterion, introduced
Mrs. Molyncux—wife of Dr. Molyneux, lately of the P. & O.
Company—to a Sydney audience, which obviously liked
the lady. Mrs. M. is tall and fair, and walks the stage
like an artist, and if she only wouldn’t emphaticize every
second word her method would be very bearable—her
love-making being realistic enough to send a surge of old
remembrance over the soul of the apparently unfledged
d.e. seraph. “ Isn’t it just like the way one docs?” said
one rapturously, but unguardedly, to a chum the other
night when Mrs. M. dropped her head into Basil’s shirt-
front. Miss Romer was quite perfect as the worldly’
aristocrat with a hark strangely at variance with her Lite*
so to speak, and her dresses were in excellent taste, the
grey brocade and white silk garment, with bonnet of grey
tulle, pink roses, and diamond pins, worn in act 2>
especially. But how Mr. Anson (you can’t help watering
jour smile at his “ Bunter,” with a regretful tear that
we are going to lose our little Eccles) can look such a
sloping-shouldered slop-fitted scarecrow, is one of the

Apropos theatres, men who take a party of ladies of un-
certain age to one of these should be careful not to reserve
chairs with numbers in any waj’ coinciding with the ages
of the women. Because the ushers are sure to shriek
them out as they pilot the party to their perches, and the
figures sometimes fit the the individuals like a glove. For
instance, at the Garrick, the other night, a man turned
up squiring a bevy of women (who didn’t look as if they
had come down in the last shower), and as they ranged
along the gangway with the eye of the house upon them
—“Thirty-seven to forty!” screamed the usher. I
wouldn’t have been one of them for the world.

At an inquest held on the 11th March at
Prahran, (Vic.), on a very small infant of sixteen
months, the mother of the deceased cheerfully
attested that he had never been suckled, but had
been fed on “rump-steak, brandy, mutton-broth,”
and various other sundries. An infant who starts
life on rump-steak and brandy, and hangs out for
sixteen months under these conditions, must be
an exceedingly tough offspring.
An exchange recalls the killing of “ Wel-
lington,” probably the biggest giant among Aus-
tralian black fellows since the advent of the white
man to this continent. “Wellington’' was 7ft.
high, and weighed, in the nude, over 17-stone. He
was a chief of one of the Nicholson River (N.Q.)
tribes, and a noted terror to whites, with whom
housed to “gammon friends ” previous to acting
treacherously. On the day of his death, he had
crossed the Nicholson to a white's camp, when a
“dispersing party” that had been trailing him
came up. “Wellington” plunged back into the
river, and was scrambling up the opposite bank
when a “ Winchester ” pinged him, apparently in
the shoulder. Though he gave a terrific bound
and ran for nearly a quarter of a mile, it was
afterwards discovered that the bullet bad gone
clean through the centre of his heart.
Dear Bulletin,-- Be the discussed scandal-
ous treatment of common soldiers by their officers
in Germany, I have a cousin who has served
his time in the Prussian army, and he informs me
that the instances quoted in the press are not only
true but far below the average. He told me a
number of j ar.is, most of them being rather too
“steep’’for publication, but a couple of humorous
incidents occurred among them. One day the
lieutenant of a certain regiment called a private,
but the man, instead of walking directly to him,"
stepped a little on one side and picked up a button
from the ground cn route. “ Ho, ho, my friend,”
exclaimed the officer, “is that the way you march !
Write, by six o’clock to-morrow morning, 500
times, “a straight line is the nearest means of
approach between two given points.” Anyone
who has been for days engaged in German musket
and general drill practice will realise what sort of
a job this would prove to ids tired fingers.

by order of the captain there will be an eclipse of

therefoie will take place during that interval.

down deceased, and his father-in-law sluL.
his wife, and he loses all his money, qv*
he is further worried in the third act became
he and his wife are both starving in a moiildv
room in a tenement house, and he can get m,
work, and the landlady threatens to throw them
out, and they have to borrow food from his father’s
first wife’s first husband, who is in rags ■ ami
George is assaulted by his hereditary enemy,’after
which a bale of wool falls on Ids stomach m a
dock. He doesn’t brighten up much in Act IV
for the sufficient reason that he is in the hospital
with a broken arm and squashed chest, and he
has no prospects, and his wife is starving and lost
and insane, and he is hooted at in his misery by
Ids father’s first wife. Also, he looks depressed
in Act V., because, though he has recovered
the property, Ids own wife is still lost—she
is found at last partially deranged behind a shrub.
Then William Rignold—as George Rignold’s
father-in-law—is sorrowful because he has gnashed
his teetli in a pasteboard cottage for 18 years, and
foamed, and lashed out in impotent fury, and
when at last his vengeance comes he finds it has
gone bad. Mr. Jewett is melancholy because a
tramp is perpetually threatening to have him
hanged ; Mr. Carey is sad because he has been in
Siberia; Miss Watt-Pldllips is gloomy and tear-
sodden because she has been cursed by her father,
and hasn’t enjoyed a square meal ever since she
married Mr. Rignold; and Miss Editli Bland
fails to keep her spirits up for various reasons,
and ultimately goes into a convent. r J here is not
one gleam of sunshine in the whole five acts. The
entire show is a heap of groaning ruins wallowing
in a sea of grief, and the one spot in it which is
not so dark as the rest is an old woman who is
stone-broke, and expects to go to the workhouse,
and thinks from his shrieks that her husband has
fallen into the fire downstairs.
# * *
Under these painful conditions there is
not much to be said for the company. William
Rignold is by far the best feature in it; beads
well, and his head looms up further above the
cataract of woe than the head of anybody else.
Mr. Jewett’s villain has become stereotyped; he
sneers with a certain old, disused sneer, and even
when you only see his feet under a gate you know
diat he will come to a bad end. This expectation
is fully realised, for George Rignold mops him all
over the place in the last scene, and strangles him,
and tears him through shrubs by the head, and
then he is arrested and taken away for execution.
George Rignold does well, but ho is loaded
down with tons of anguish, and there are
whole mountains on his head. About once in
every two acts he comes to the surface of his
troubles to breathe, and then he dives again and
flops about wearily at the bottom of an ocean cf
misery. Miss Edith Bland, as the boss adven-
turess, has a tendency to be hard and wooden, and
moreover she is objectionable as being the cause
of the whole trouble; she sowed an infant when
she was young, and the seed falling on responsive
soil she reaps Mr. Jewett in her middle age.
George Carey and Miss Bessie Rignold are two
redeeming features, and the rest is more grief and
much scenery. “Life’s Rough Road” is too
suicidal, too damp, too depressing—except, cf
course, for that large class of persons who like to
take their woe neat. An ordinary man might
send his creditors and his enemies and his mother-
in-law to see it, but he could hardly desire to see
it himself.

That faith which is commonly personified
by a picture of a green, damp female clinging tea
sea-washed rock has kept “A Sailor’s Knot”
booming for many days at Sydney Royal, hut its
end is now at hand. And before it goes we desire
to ask Air. Bland Holt, in a loud voice, to patch
up one weak point which appears in all his dramas,
and in this one a little more, perhaps, than in the
rest, and to put the patch on before his next pro-
duction appears. We refer to the utter inadequacy
of the exp’anation offered by the man who rushes
in just when the hero is about to be hanged, and
saves him from destruction. All through the first
four acts, as a rule, the leading character is
trodden underfoot by brigands, hangmen, and
wild spielers, he loses his girl, he is thrown into
gaol and thrown out again, and finally a
crisis arrives when he is going to he
finally obliterated. And just here it generally
happens that a thTd party gallops in, and offers
an unsupported statement which has nothing at
all to do with the matter in hand, and having
thrown this fact on the troubled waters every-
thing comes right. Practically the dramatist
travels on the assumption that 'when his wicked
individual has crawled through a s ea of infamy to
gain a certain purpose, he will naturally collapse
the instant some nnshoin spectre comes in head
first, and clears up the mystery which surrounded
something else—the loss of an umbrella, period
25 years previously. This sort of thing burdens
our soul. Unless the man who rushes in 9® 11
come armed with a more reasonable explanation
the heio’s execution had la Iter proceed.

Mr. Alfred Tennyson Dickens is still
stumping the suburbs of Sydney in a dreary way
with his dreary lecture about his father. It is a
sad thing to hear a lachrymose man in a black
coat yearning miserably over a dead genius, and
we devoutly trust it may never happen again.
** * *
At Sydney Criterion, “ New Men and Old
Acres” is drawing its last breath and nightly
orotfds. The public appreciation of Anson’s
Kenischun Banter has both elongated and
widened with each performance, and the sterling,
old stall-fed classic reveals its honoured streakiness
of humour and pathos. The only change in the
wist has been the substitution of Clitheroe, as
Seeker, the attorney, for Lash Gordon, who was
wanted at Melbourne Bijou. Mrs. Molyneux’s
Lilian Vavasour is full of power and esprit. Miss
K. Homer makes a properly disgusting and arti-
ficial English “ me-lady.” Miss Brenda Gibson,
as Fanny Bunter, carries out the unpedigreed
languishing of the unutterable yearn with under-
standable effect. Miss Annie Taylor, as Mrs.
Banter, cockers along vulgarly in orthodox (stage)
parvenu style. Robert Brough is Marmaduke
Vavasour, and—Robert Brough. Sass is a self-
contained self-made man and nature's gentleman
as Sam. Brown, and H. Chichester, as Bertie
Fitz-Urse, bubbles with hobbledehoy enthusiasm
and younger-son’s recklessness. “Caste” is
the bill-of-fare for the coming week, with Anson
as Eccles.

Go sober to Sydney Royal Standard
Theatre while Mons. D’Arc holds the board 8
there with his Marionettes and his Fani\chc s
Fran<;aise. Mock gymnasts and rope-walkers, of
both sexes, tipsy jugglers and serpent-men, song*
and-dance artistes, and Bluebeard and his retinue,
carry on in apparent defiance of all natural laws,
tumble to pieces, and chase with one part of their
anatomies several other parts, and mysteriously
reunite their fragments ; and a transformation
scene, full of fiery dragons and dazzling fays and
orange waterfalls like nothing under the sun,
except a bacchanal’s loosened brainspring, gives
an ecstatic finish to a bewildering phantasmagoria.
Go sober, we say ; and go home sober and take a
sedative draught.

Saturday’s revival of “ Dorothy,’ Mel-
bourne Princess’s, was a snapless function, for the
’"ost part owing to a complication of reasons
which didn’t always appear upon the surface of
'he show. When Lurcher Elton was falling
downstairs,- or being reduced to rags by a gaudy
peasantry, matters seemed to progress as of yore,
ut when that immortal writ-server retired to the
y llr ‘ lo lelt a larger vacuum than usual. Howard
ernon, another “ Dorothy” identity, compared
avourably with his former self; also Ryley (as
I man who sings an inflammatory ballad
° Miss Bantam at her bedroom threshold) left
wtlung in particular to be desired. The interest
novelty was attached to five of the principals,
and of these the two with least to do—Mira
•uneron and Miss Yarley—came off fairly well
n the struggle with early “Dorothy ” traditions.

vivacity in either the music, the comedy,
both. Miss Marie Halton is physically and

of an English country squire, one of the

“Carmen,” now in rehearsal, will be wanted
very shortly.

Mrs. Bernard-Bcere, at the Opera House,
does her best to conceal the fact that she ought to
be taking a long holiday from stage-business, but
there is, unhappily, no strength in the effort. The
gifted lady grew more wan and weary as last week
wore away, and the general opinion of “As in a
Looking Glass ” was influenced accordingly.
Nothing short of Mrs. Beere at her beA could
suffice to keep such a play in great favour, and so
the bill is to bo changed on Saturday, when,
health permitting, the “star” will appear as Peg
Woffington.

Two benefit performances filled the Royal
comfortably last Saturday. There was a matinee
on behalf of Miss Claire Duvalli, an alleged Melba
of the future, whose organ is to undergo a course
of Italian treatment. Anon Billee Barlow had her
night out, appearing as Nan, in “Good for
Nothing,” and washing her face amidst many
manifestations of joy. Billee is now about to
pack up her theatrical wardrobe in a small
black bag, and leave the boys lamenting.
She has clone a good deal for the Royal
in its upper storeys, and her tights will
long be talked of by all who knew them. J. F.
Sheridan enters into possession of the house forth-
with, and intends to break out anew as Bridget
O’Brien, Esquire, instead of fulfilling a fearful
threat to paralyse them with “The Shaughraun.”
** * *
“ Sophia ” occupies the boards of Mel-
bourne Bijou in the meantime, The hero, Jones,
is once again professing his ability to work, if the
worst comes to the worst. Partridge is around,
too, came as ever, and the purification of poor
Tom’s other shirt is still a soapy feature of the
entertainment. There is far too much room to
spare in the Bijou auditorium of late, but in the
general ability of the performances there are no
gaps, and the glory of the accessories fades not
at all. Everything is calculated to please,
with the exception of the waits, which
are apt to be hideously long, and gratuitously
tiresome. In these times of depression people
mustn’t be left to sit and think for 15 consecutive
minutes in a quiet, sparsely-populated theatre,
because they are sure to think that they wish they
hadn’t come. However, “ Sunlight and Shadow ”
(on April 2nd) will be as new to Melbourne as
Miss Ida Liston, and the vision of a strange
actress in a strange play should arouse a burst of
attention.
* Hf * *
“Wilful Murder” appeals to the blood-
shot eye of a horror-stricken community, and the
crime on the Alexandra hearthstone “has an
extraordinary attraction for ladies ” who come in
theatre parties to the feast of gore and shudder as
though they liked it. The villain of the piece is a
distinct libel on “Williams,” ina*much as his
lovemaking would put a female elephant on her
guard, and his appearance calls for vengeance all
the time. As a throat-cutter, however, he has
never been equalled. The weird whistle
of the razor, severing the arteries of his
bonny bride, breaks upon the breathless silence
with such ghastly significance that the victim’s
head is confidently expected to drop off and roll
into the new-made grave. At this point the
illusion would be quite complete if the fiend
were provided with a penny squirt to scatter blood
with. Many other opportunities are missed in
“ Wilful Murder ” which is poorly stage-managed,
but the overthrow of “Williams” in the last act
brings this tropical show to a striking conclusion.

Afc the Athenaeum a savant described as
Professor John is magnifying microbes and in-
visibles of all sorts to five million times their
natural size, so that the everlasting struggle of
animal life may be studied upon a large canvas.
At a lot of other shows the same struggle may bo
observed with the naked eye.
At Poverty Point.
Dear Bulletin ; “ The gruesome story told in a recent
issue about the hull which carried off a black baby impaled
on its horns, and wandered round with this depressing
burden until the bull went mad and the baby fell into
decay, suggests a good idea for Dampier’s next melodrama.
The great trouble in melodrama is to find some new and
original way of disposing of the villain. Therefore, I
would suggest to Garnet Walch that that base indhidual,
just when he reaches the climax of his crimes in the third
act, should be rushed from behind by a frenzied cow, and
carried away impaled and shrieking horribly. Then the
fourth and fifth acts could he devoted to putting things
right in a sylvan glade, and every now and then the cow
could rush’madly past with the decaying remains of the
bad man trailing from its horns. Regarded as a means of
appealing to the inner soul of the gallery this ought to
take well and give pleasure to all.— Titus Salt.
Odell, a splendid comedian, is said to he coming out to
Australia in search of an engagement. For years past, he
nas been one of the neglected geniuses of Ihe purfesh,
and a few friends and admirers are raising the necessary
wind to waft him to these parts. A hundred good yarns
are told of Odell’s humour and impecuniosily. His
regular attendance at a club where his subscription was
about three years overdue was considered such a shock-
ing example that thfc management at last served him
with a sharp notice to keep away. A night or two after-
wards the irrepressible broker strolled in as usual and
ordered a frugal banquet. The manager was sent for,
and he requested that the gentleman would pficefnlly
retire. “It’s all right,” said Odell, “this is my Last
Supper, but the twelve have not turned up.”|
Max Klein, who first came to Melbourne as first violin
in the Gowen orchestra, is off to the South hf Europe for
the benefit of his health.
No matter whether it be fine or wet, we always play to
crowded audiences ; we always please our kind patrons,
and always receive the most flattering notices from a dis-
criminating Press; and thus encouraged by the generous
approval of the public, endorsed by the critics, the worry
and anxiety of running so large a company is to us a
labour of love, rendered comparatively light since we
possess the agreeable conviction that we bask in the
genial sunshine of the good opinion of those whom it is
the actor’s vocation to please— to live.”— Extract from
Dan. Dairy's handbill, addressed “To (ha Generous
Public of Australasia” out in the wilderness.

Lawrence’s long-promised “Life of G. V. Brooke” is
at last criticised by the London papers, and in circulation.
The lady who accompanied Brooke to Melbourne as his
wife was a Miss Bray, of Birmingham. After the wreck of
the London a bottle was found at Brighton with a mes-
sage from Brooke to Avonia Jones in it on a torn envelope.
Brooke’s letters from Melbourne are deeply interesting.
Robert Brough, of Brough and Boucioault, writes a
sensible letter to the Era about Tom Squire, the Gaiety
dancer, who died in Melbourne, leaving a wife and four
children. Squire, his wife and family, were sent out from
England by a theatrical subscription, and Brough shows
the folly of this course, under the circumstances. Another
subscription was raised among the profession to send Mrs.
Squire and her children back to London.
A daughter of Mr. Call, the Melbourne ex-P.M., is on
the London stage, in the “Great Metropolis,” at the
Princess’s. She acts as Miss Waud, hut is a widow, dis-
tinguished as an amateur. Her mother, a lady of strong
literary tastes, was unfortunately burnt to death at Caul-
field, Melbourne.
The limelight went wrong during Mrs. Bernard Beere’s
death-scene, the other night, and she had to expire in the
darkness. Probably the fair invalid was glad of the
opportunity to take things easy in her last moments.
Ladies will be shocked to hear that Charles Ryley
suffered from two or three genuine fainting-fits on the
stage last week. Ryley, indeed, is quite, “run down,”
and the charmers who used to pelt him with floral
tributes might well give a practical turn to their adora-
tion by chucking on some champagne-jelly or—shall we
say it ?—Clements Tonic.
Herbert Standing’s voice seems so hard and inflexible,
in villain parts, that only these who have heard his
“imitations” can believe him to be a prince of mimics.

a Bulletin correspondent, is “The-New Wing,” with
which Willie Edouin has “ struck ile,” at the Strand. It
is good from start to finish, and is clever without being
vulgar. Willie Edouin himself, as a ’onest working
decorator, is perfectly killing. In thecourse of onescene he
papers a whole room in a style which would make Roth
green with envy. It is said that he worked in a paper-
hanger’s shop for several hours a day for a month to learn
the business.

When necessary Sells’ circus can pitch its great tent for
receiving an audience in less than an hour. The country
performances generally terminate at 10.30, and by mid-
night the big show is often packed up and away on its
journey to fresh fields.
Dear Bulletin, —It was years ago at Climes (Vie.). A
few of us (waifs and strays of the Company, frozen
out at the old Royal, Ballarat) had gone there as a forlorn
hope. Opened at the Apollo. Can’t say if it was our
want of quality, or the opposition of the Novaros and
Charlie Lascelles at the Town Hall; anyway, the “ghost
didn’t walk.” Stayed at B. & M. Hotel. Had come, but
couldn’t go. Landlord froze on to traps. Manageress
went to Ballarat to try to raise the wind. Pretty hope-
less that. However, got the ladies away, so didn’t care
much. Landlord very affable; money sure to come.
Some days elapsed ; things looking bluer. Happy thought:
go up town, engage room ; quiet place—very. Drizzly
weather ; overcoats on, of course. Always used to knock
about. Anxiously awaiting that money ; also, cf course,
used to drop into hotel and wander about aimlessly. Go
upstairs : one pair tights, two ditto pants, sundry vests,
one or more coats, sundries in pockets -Overcoat.
Gentle stroll up town. Into that quiet room: disrobe.
Da capo. Ha! Horrid thought! 1 Weight of boxes
Hum. Happy idea: a screw in floor. Act last. — First
opening of Climes railway. Naturally go and look on.
Besides, expect those funds. Lots of people about. Stroll
about. Go home to hotel. Landlord gone by train to
Ballarat to look up that money. Afternoon train.
Nobody about. Bundles hastily fetched from quiet room.
Exeunt (mines per train. Horrid thought again: land-
lord at Ballarat Station. Misgivings. (Play Jacques
Strop well now—“Oh, my poor nerves!”) “Ballarat,
Ballarat.” Not here ! Joy !! Safe ! !! No more perfesh
for me, thank you.— The Palmer.

pondent) that after all the fuss and bother Nellie Stewart
should have been almost a failure. The ciilics showed
her little mercy, and damned her with faint praise when

ami on the first night, Arthur Williams says, she quite

The British “ Medical Council ” recently
struck the name of a surgeon-dentist off the
register because he advertised in the newspapers,
and (he British law-courts have formally decided
they were fully justified in doing so, and that
their conduct was perfectly correct. This is the
sort of professional despotism which will be es-
tablished in N.S. Wales whenever the Medical
Bill gets through the Legislature. There was no
claim put forward that the person in question was
a bad dentist—in fact he seems to have been an
exceptionally good one. The public didn’t object
to him advertising, and as they kept him in fees
they were the only individuals who had any right
to object. The Council of the Medical Register
didn’t pay him, and therefore they had no more
right to interfere with his advertising than they
had to prevent him washing his head. The
Council wouldn’t have said anything if he had
been a drunkard, or if he had been an unskilful
operator and had broken a patient’s jaw—these
things only concern that great, dull, patient,
enduring ass, the general public, and it doesn’t
matter in the least. But when he trod on the
“etiquette of the profession”—which doesn't
concern the public in the slightest degree—then
the medical volcano woke up on the spot.
The special reporter in W.A. of (he S.M.
Herald wires that Deeming confessed to his
solicitor, Mr. R. S. Haynes, that lie was guilty of
the Rainhill murders and also of some of the
“Ripper” crimes. Now, in West Australia, as
in most parts of the civilised world, it is the
privilege of an accused man under lock-and-key to
confer with his legal adviser in absolute privacy ;
therefore, assuming the truth of the Herald's
allegation (which is strenuously denied by Mr.
Haynes), it is clear that either Deeming or his
attorney must have been the original means of
promulgating the report printed in the Herald —a
report, by the way, which has been universally
circulated and which renders it absolutely impos-
sible for Deeming to have a fair trial anywhere in
Australasia at any time within thenextl2 months.
Apropos Mr. Haynes’ disclaimer, The Bulletin
may remark that if it could be proved that any
lawyer engaged in a prisoner’s defence had broken
that prisoner's confidence, he would and should
be struck off the rolls. And, be it said to the
credit of the legal profession, there is, so far as wo
know, no recorded instance, either in England,
America, or Australia, of any lawyer having
unauthorisedly divulged his client’s confession—
any more than there is on record any case wherein
a minister of the Gospel has betrayed his trust
in such a way.

derer,
And, search the earth entirely through, you
couldn’t get no “ furderer ”;
I have a constant weakness for a-varying of my
missis, sir,
I never stop at anything from cutting throats to

And when I’m tired of honeymoons, would plunge
some new adventure in,
I basil a skull and cut a throat, and brick and
then cement her in.
I love the girls, as any man who’s fond of marriage
bans should do;
I keep my family worries dark, as every married
man should do;
I close the door on every male who visits my mia-
mia and rings,
And ere I cut my “ present’s ” throat I bait my
“next ” with diamond-rings ;
No girls I’ve met resist that lure unique in all its
“only-ness,”
And then I clinch it with my sighs, my love, and
pitied “loneliness.”
I never do let go my hold, nor let my love get

And as I keep the dead-’uns’ gems, my jewels
don’t get backwarder;

By heedless generosity I pick up all the blos-
som ers ;

It is generally believed that the late Duke
ot Clarence’s affection for Princess May was
of very new growth ; that his first and only love
was the Princess Helene, of Orleans, daughter of
the Comte de Paris. The story goes that when
he was on his death-bed, he called out, “Helene,
Helene, where arc you?” He continued this for
so long that it was considered advisable to send
Princess May out of the room.
Mrs. Humphrey Ward, of “ Robert
Eismere” fame, is a grand-daughter of Colonel
Sorell, one of the first governors of Tasmania.
Tom Arnold, her father, was a son of old Dr.
Arnold, of Rugby. The Sorells are all noted for
their furious tempers. When Tom Arnold turned
Roman Catholic, his wife (a Sorell) went and
smashed the windowsof St. Joseph’s R.C. Church,
Hobart, and kicked up a terrible how-d’ye-do.
One of Spurgeon’s stock anecdotes. He
once met a labouring man shoving a big barrow
uphill, and perspiring profusely as be did so.
“It’s a hot day, my friend,” said tbe sympathetic
preacher. “ Yes, it is,” said the artizan, briefly,
“ and I never seed a man look more like standing
a pint of beer than you in my life.” Spurgeon
told that story at least fifty times, and yet he
died without once mentioning whether he stood
the pint of beer or not.
The Tasmanian Chief Justice (Sir Lambert
Dobson) says he does not believe in the “ bar-
barously long sentences sometimes inflicted,” and
thinks that short sentences meet most cases quite
as effectively as long ones. This is the judge who
was guilty—upon his own confession the other
day—of the heinous offence of giving, in the Port
Arthur days, pieces of tobacco to stray prisoners
whom he came across during his visits to the
“ convict establishment.” Shades of all the
“Commandants,” just think of it!
It is no secret that ever since the Tranby
Croft affair the relations between the Prince and
Princess of Wales have been strained. This did
not arise from the gambling incident, but from a
circumstance which was hinted at, but suppressed
at the time. It is common talk in London that
when the Princess last left England, ostensibly to
visit her sister, it was her intention not to return
to England. The illness of Prince George induced
her to come back suddenly, and so what promised
to be a very grave scandal was avoided.
Prince Alexander of Holland, tire second
son of the mad, dirty, snuffy old King William
who died a year or so ago, used to dress himself,
in his later years, in orange coloured petticoats
and a bustle and the other items of female costume.
Influential visitors who were introduced at Court
used to be a good deal staggered when they met a
crooked, warped, gnarled-looking, middle-aged
hag with an old, old, bleary eye and a hide-bound
expression and a glaring yellow frock, and were
told that this horrid apparition was a prince and
heir-presumptive to the throne of the Netherlands.
Alexander was buried in his female costume, and
bis aged father spilt his snuff helplessly into bis
grave; and then the Prince of Orange drank
himself into the same tomb, and the inebriated
monarch followed them, and the three old frumps
sleep together iu the royal mausoleum at Delft.
