A sailor called at a holding on the Lachlan
River, owned by an eccentric old fellow nick-
named “’Cute-Eye,” asked for work, and was
engaged to drive a team consisting of one horse in
the shafts and two bullocks in tbe lead. All went
well until Jack was returning with a. dray-load of
wood, when, on coming through the gate, he hit
one of the posts. The wood slipped back and
tipped ttie dray up, and the horse hung up in the
belly-band. The bullocks turned the yoke and
faced the horse, and the near-side bullock got on
the off-side, and the off-side bullock got on the
near-side. “ ’Cuto-Eyo ” came running up, and
exclaimed “What is the matter, my man?’
“Why, can’t you see?” said the sailor, “the
port bullock has got on the starboard side, and
the blooming horse has gone up the rigging.”
An Aboriginal Othello.
A YARN FROM SPINIFEX LAND.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
There is one thing about the outskirts of civili-
sation in Australia that does not come up to
generally-entertained ideas. Gambling is not
indulged in to the extent that some wnteis of
fiction would pretend. When I use the phrase
‘‘gambling” I mean card-playing. 1 here is a
great deal of play, but the stakes are never ruinous
nor are there any professional gamblers. A
“Jack Hamlin” is unknown, or he takes the
form of an obscure “spieler ” in constant danger
of being kicked. The romantic vagabond of Bret
Hai tc s creation does not exist. I’or all that, i
remember a strange stake being played for once,
and that stake cost the winner bis life.
I was down with the fever at the tune in fact,
everyone on the station had it more or less. 1 or-
tnnately, we all had our bad days and our good
days, and as they did not always fall together
there was generally somebody available to do the
necessary work.
The head station was 27 miles away, lucre
were four of us and a black boy on this out-station,
and on the night I write of two of the fellows
were camped out, I was down on my bunk with
the shakes, and Blake, who was all right, was
wondering how the deuce he was to kill the
evening. , . , ,
Samson, the black boy, was rather a remarkable
nigger. He was not tall, but, for breadth and
muscle he was a perfect Hercules; be was a
splendid rider, and had been partially educated
when a boy. He was an old policeman, and
escaped, half-dead, from the bands of the Uawson
blacks the night his more unfortunate officer got
his skull battered in. He turned out too insubor-
dinate for the police, and drifted into statio
employment.
“ I think I’ll get Samson in to play euchre,
said Blake, pricking up the drooping wick of the
slush-lamp. “It won’t disturb you, will it ?”
I intimated that at that particular peiiod 1
didn’t care if he got Setebos in to play euchre witli
him, and presently Samson, who, as an old police-
man, was, of course, a good euchre-player, was
seated in the hut playing with Blake.
How long they played I don’t know. I went off
into one of those halt-sleeps common to malarial
fever, and when I awoke I was in a gentle per-
spiration ; the languid feeling that always follows
the ague was on me, and there seemed no pleasure
on earth like that of lying on my hard bunk and
lazily dozing.
Blake and the boy were still playing. In a
dreamy kind of way I could make out that; also
they were quarrelling over their cards, and a name
was being bandied about. This was the name of
Samson’s gin, a handsome native woman belong-
ing to the district, generally supposed to be a half-
caste, the offspring of some shipwrecked sailor;
for these wore the days when the Far North was
first settled.
“ Monday,” which was the name of the gin, as
on that day the had been captured by Samson,
was a remarkable-looking woman. Unlike the
general run of the natives she had good features
and well-formed limbs ; she was c qiper-coloured,
not black. Samson was madly jealous of her ; and
it was with surprise, even in my lethargic state,
that I heard Blake say, “ Very well, let’s play for
Monday ! ”
I went off into a beautiful sleep, one that made
amends for all the rending and tearing of the ague.
I woke up to the noise of much swearing and
loud talk going on outside. The hut was nearly
dark, the wick of the slush-lamp was flickering to
its end. I gathered my blanket around me and
went out on my shaky legs to see what the trouble
was.
Samson was standing at the door of his grass
hut, hurling choice patois at poor Monday, who
had been rudely thrust out and her blankets
hurled after her. Blake was trying to interfere
and stop the quarrel.
“ What’s the matter?” I asked, with chattering
teeth.
“O, nothing. Samson kept losing,” returned
Blake, “and he proposed playing for Monday; 0 t
course I won, and now the d d fool takes it in
earnest, although I keep telling him it was only a
joke.’'
“ Come back to bed,” I said, “ and it will be all
right in the morning.”
Samson had somewhat subsided on hearing my
voice, and Blake went back with me into the
hut, in fact, had to half lift me to my bunk, f or
the night air had brought the ague on again, and
I went straight away into delirium.
I have often tried to recall the dreams of the
four-and-twenty hours that followed, but cannot
in any way connect them with the events that
really took place. At last I awoke to the fact
that somebody was holding my head up, and
trying to give me some broth with a teaspoon, j
opened my dull eyes and recognised in my voiuffi
nurse, Jim Brown, one of the two men who had
been camped out on the night in question. Tlic
fever had left me, and after a good drink of broth
I sat up and looked around. Both Brown and
Jackson (the other man) regarded me somewhat
curiously.
“ Did you hear anything of it?” said Brown at
last. “ You must have had a narrow squeak
yourself.”
“ Hear anything?” I echoed stupidly.
“When we came home,” said Brown, “ We
spotted niggers’ tracks all round the place. Blake
was lying down at the waterhole with his head
smashed in, and two empty buckets alongside
him. He had been killed while going down for
water. We found Monday with her head caved
in, but Samson’s body we have not found yet.
How, in the name of fortune, did they spare
you?”
I put my hands to my head and tried to think
and at last it came back to me, and I told the
other fellows about the game of euchre and the
silly joke that Samson had taken in earnest.
“ We’ll find him with the niggers,” said
Jackson. “And the sooner we start after thein
the better,” he added, grimly.
By the time the fresh horses came in I was able
to get about and determined to go with them.
The excitement gave me a little strength back.
It took us two days to track those niggers up,
and finally we caught them in a gorge. Samson
might have played up with us for he had
taken his carbine with him, but the fool had been
shooting kangaroos for the blacks, and when we
overtook them ho had not got a cartridge left,
I never found out how the niggers turned up so
quickly that time, unless they came with the in-
tention of attacking the hut, and Samson some-
how managed to make friends with them first;
anyhow, they let me off cheap.
BINGHI.
Francis Adams’ Fortnightly article robs
his judgments of value because of Ids painfully
laboured attempts at phrase-making, and portrait-
drawing with epigram. There are no half-tones
in his pictures, and the perspective is horribly
distorted. It is not the fidelity of the portraits,
but .the circumstances of location that identify
Lane, of the Worker, as “Laurie,” and David
Syme as “the Australian Greeley.” As to Pai-kea,
Adams has failed to grasp the one characteristic
of the man which explains both his successes and
his failures—-an enormous mental receptivity
operating with the vanity of the crude, self taught
man. And, with respect to Griffith and
M'llwraith, events, while the magazine was on its
way out here, have damned both the criticised and
the critic.
What People Say.
MRS: A. PREATT, ROWNTREE-STREET,
BALMAIN, N.S.W.
Writes : —"Having been a victim to advanced
liver disease for years, and tried all the medical
treatment the neighbourhood affords, I am only
too happy to bear witness to the wonderful results
obtained by the use of Clements Tonic. I have
been an inmate of the Balmain Cottage Hospital,
and have had the experience and attention of
every doctor in Balmain, being under their treat-
ment for months, all without the slightest benefit.
I suffered with chronic constipation, complicated
with an anal fistula, which rendered the action of
the bowels impossible without the use of instru-
ments, and the torturing was unbearable. I was
a martyr to headaches, always languid, tired, and
excessively weak, no inclination or ability to move
about, and totally unable to do any housework for
the last two years. I commenced the use of
Clements Tonic, and before I was through the
second bottle, the effect was really marvellous. I
could eat, work, and sleep, my pains and aches
ceased ; I felt a different woman entirely. My
bowels acted freely, and without any of the intense
pain I used to suffer. I have not the slightest
hesitation in stating that Clements Tonic is a
most wonderful and effective remedy ; it has cured
me, and made me strong again, when doctors and
hospitals have failed, t am only too pleased to
give my to timony in favour of Clements Tonic,

Patriotic League,” finding that it grew

Col. Templeton (of Melbourne) :
In connection with the Premier Permanent Building
Society prosecutions, the then Attorney-General ordered
that a warrant which had been issued for the arrest of a
director should not lie executed. I saw on that occasion
the anomaly of a detective having a warrant for the
arrest of a person, and hiding himself from the man
whom it was his duty to apprehend, because a Minister
had directed that the offender should not be arrested.
The total value of N.S.W. lands held in
private ownership is £178,500,000. Half of these
alienated lands are held by 520 persons, who own
more than half their value, since they have the
pick of the lot. Again, 656 freeholders, outside
of the boundaries of cities, towns and townships,
possess among them 20,847,216, or 50.65 per cent
of the total area ( outside , die.) alienated from the
State.
Australasian Pastoralists’ Review says
that the stoppage of Kanaka labor in Queensland
was, “in a measure, due to Sir Samuel Griffith.”
This is the unkindliest cut of all. Nine years ago,
Sir Sam. was the animating soul of the whole
anti-Kanaka movement, the public denouncer of
the South Sea Slave-trade, and the “Our Sam.”
of the masses. Now, he is the apostate who has
turned Blackbirder himself.
A very few years ago Griffith announced,
at a public meeting, in Brisbane, that should
black-labour triumph he would leave Queensland
with his family, as that country would then bo
unfit for a white man to live in. This leaves just
two alternatives; either Griffith isn’t a white
man, or else he is the sort of white man who is
eminently fitted to live in places that aren’t fit for
any other kind of white man to live in.
A surreptitious Chinaman sneaks into
Queensland from the Northern Territory. The
border police catch him and run him back some
miles. “ Welly good.” Johnny goes serenely on
for a day, camps for a week, and trots back into
Queensland. He is caught again, and fined £5O
or six months. “Also welly good; likewise no
savee ; sposee no gotee, how can payee ?” So the
yellow-skin “goes up;” loafs on Government for
six months, and thus earns the right to a free run
all over Queensland.

thing, especially as the League had rented the
hall. “lam in the place where,” &c. Bah !

Sydney D.T. leading article
The glamour raised bv the advocates of Protection has
already passed away. There is neither sign of the up-
springing of new industries nor of a revival of trade.
The clamours of the N.S.W. unemployed are as loud as
ever and their numbers are still large.
So very like the Telegraph. N.S. Wales tried
Foreign-trade for over 50 years, and the millen-
nium didn’t come. It has now tried Protection—
in a very milk-and-water way—for three months
and the millennium hasn’t come either. And
because mild Protection hasn’t done in three
months what cast-iron Foreign-trade didn't do in
50 years, therefore Protection is utterly bad and
Foreign-trade is superlatively good.
“ Did you ever know an election which did
not involve the death of the Government which
conducted it?” asks the Kapunda Herald (S.A.).
We have— now and then, but very, very seldom.
As an almost invariable rule the Government goes
smash at a general election because it is so utterly
rotten and bad that the country thinks it can’t
get a worse one. Therefore, it puts the other
party in, and at the earliest opportunity it bounces
it out again on the ground that it is also so bail
that it can’t be worse. And having bounced
out the second party, the electors go back to the
first one. and find it no better than it was before.
The choice lies between the devil the country
knows and the devil it has partly forgotten about,
and they sit on the nation’s chest turn-about.
Black-labour is a cumulative disease.
Many of its Queensland votaries are now dis-
seminating the theory that, if sugar-planters are
allowed to employ Kanakas, all classes of
employers should be permitted to enjoy (!) the
same privilege (!). That evidences the danger to
white workers, whose duty is to damn the slave-
trade every time it is advocated, to pill it at every
ballot-box, to bash it crushingly every time it
rears its ugly snout above the surface of politics.
The cry may yet be given out and responded to—
“ Boston-tea the cheap Kanakas !”
Premier Shiels proposes a rule-of-thumb
system for the Victorian Civil Service reduction,
which fails altogether to meet the case. He tried
at first to make the departmental heads propose
the reductions, but they wouldn’t. The increment
and pension system is a remnant of the era when
O’Shanassy tried to quarter every Irishman on the
State —and then the Argyles came to the post,
but never the poor English. The high-waged
Civil-servant is a byeword for improvidence.
The tailaw, butchaw, grocaw, bakaw, are his
natural enemies, as may be seen on any fraud-
summons day.
The S.M. Herald, in a parliamentary
note ;
The object was to test the whole system of pensions,
and, of course, all the political faddists rushed at once
to the front. Mr. Allen grew excited about the matter,
and proposed to place tram-guards and lawyers on the
sune basis as regards salary and pension. That is to say,,
he does not admit that the special talent of the highly-
trained lawyer entitles him to any further consideration
than the most mechanical worker.
The tram-guard gets .£l2O a year : the Judge gets
“further consideration” to the tune of at least
£2500 annually. Why should the fat hog be fur-
ther greased, O Fairfax, at the expense of the
mere mechanical worker ?
The dignity of a king, even a dead one, is
an awful circumstance. King Charles of Wurtem-
berg —an old, drunken, pimpled monarch of un-
mentionable habits-died some months ago, and
Kaiser William of Germany was at the funeral.
All the same, Kaiser William didn’t know offici-
ally that the ancient potentate was gone until he
was informed of the circumstance by special pleni-
potentiary or ambassador, and the plenipotentiary
couldn’t turn up until his mourning and his
credentials wore ready, so for nearly two months
after the funeral, the Kaiser really didn't know
whether Charles was dead or not. But at last a
special envoy arrived with crape all over him, and
with a countenance of profound woe he handed in
a document, edged with three inches of murky
midnight, and setting forth, amid much circum-
locution, that King Charles was dead, deceased,
caved in, defunct, departed, bowled out, and a
g oner _ a l so , that he was buried. Then William
and the envoy each dropped a solemn tear at this
unexpected intelligence, and wiped it k away with
a black silk handkerchief. Next the envoy backed
out, and a minute later he came back witli white
gloves on, with no crape, and wearing an expression
of joy, and handed the emperor another document,
edged with gold and purple, announcing the
accession of the new monarch. And after that
they were both aware of the fact for the first time,
and the bill for this show was about .£BOO.
“ India, which before the British occupa-
tion —Bradlaugh told me-presented the har-
monious condition of universal industry ramifying
in all directions, has greatly changed. Britain,
after destroying all the national industries, has
appropriated all the profits created by India,
lienee, there prevails throughout India a state of
ever-deepening misery, whereof the world has
never afforded a more fearful example. And this
misery is not due to the conduct of the natives,
but to the deplorable English system. Between
1802 and 1854, thirteen famines swept away five
millions of human beings. Between 1860 and
1879, sixteen famines- earned off twelve millions.
The entire human race has lost, from 1793 to 1890,
in all wars : 4,500,000 units. India, in 20 years,
lost by famine alone 4,349,922. Between 1877 and
1883 the total deaths reached, 30,431,182. To all
the previous causes of this “ frightful distress ” is
added another : a limited number of Hindoos are
driven to perform, unaided, the work of the
whole nation, thus bolstering up their own
position and hindering their brethren from
earning a wretched wage. Twenty-one million
male adults are thus without employment.
In the coffee industry, for instance, that
yields England 38 millions annually, 7000 Hm-
does perform work that ought to employ 180,000
persons. In the coal industry, seven companies,.
with an aggregate capital of 10,500,000 francs,
realise annually 14 millions, while they expend,
for the wages of their 24,000 miners, barely one
million. To cap all, the national resources, to
the extent of 96’5 per cent, are in foreign hands.
Monopolies have increased by 1500 per cent, in 54
years. In 1835 they amounted to only 50 millions
of francs, in 1889 t 0750 millions.” — Martin Laya,
in Le Voyeur Illustre, April 16, 1891.
A VICTORIAN VOTER’S DILEMMA.
I’ve little faith in lawyer Shiels,
And none in Graham li.,
I’d dangle Gillies by the heels
From any shady tree ;
Alf. Deakin’s star is on the wane,
O’Loghlen is a goat;
All day I ask my puzzled brain
To tell me how to vote. .
I don’t believe in sacking Speight
(A clumsy trick) although
It really seemed that, soon or late,
His Nibs would have to go.
I do not want a tax on beer
To please the flabby “ tote.”
Election day is drawing near—
And how am I to vote?
I want my income left alone,
It wouldn’t stand a wrench.
Yet all the same I sadly groan,
“ The country must retrench.”
We need a Ministry as smart
As Joseph’s coloured coat,
But cannot get ’em, bless your heart !
And how am I to vote ?
I will not take the Argus tips
And virtues make of crimes,
I simply snort and curl my lips
At rags like Mister Syme’s.
The “fancies” of the Telegraph
Are seldom worth a groat,
Its cries excite a bitter laugh—
Then how am I to \ote?
The “ Ins ” are such a feeble push,
The “Outs” are very shy ;
This p dicy is utter both
And that is all my eye ;
But one must do the best he can
To “ keep the ship afloat”—
Trot out a decent working-man
And he shall have my vote.

In Whittakers’ Almanack for 1892,
Melbourne, Ballarat, Adelaide, Perth and Tas-
mania are given as being in Queensland !
An Adelaide hotel-landlady asks the
papers what she should do. about the teetotal
spies who hang around watching her house on
Sundays. We should do quite a lot of things.
On a broiling hot Sunday we would hire a sprinter
and let him depart with much ostentatious secresy
by the back door with a bottle of salt water
sticking out of his pocket, and after the teetotaller
— who is probably fat—has run ten miles after him
and fainted twice from sheer exhaustion we
would let him overtake his enemy and sample the
bottle. On a wet Sunday wo would fix up some
“suspicious circumstances” which would keep
the teetotallers on the qui rive so that they would
wait around in search of further evidence till they
were hopelessly soaked and suffering from rheu-
matism. Other days we would start dogs after
them, and prove subsequently, by three com-
petent witnesses, that they weren’t our dogs and
we had never seen or heard of them before. And
in between these performances we would treat the
teetotallers, and then get them disrated from the
lodge for backsliding. The landlady who doesn t
know what to do with a teetotaller on Sunday
must be a female of very little resource.
“The Lectures on the History of Litera-
ture ” by Carlyle, just published in India, have a
somewhat curious history. They were delivered
by Carlyle in ’38 to small audiences, and were, in
ail likelihood, regarded by the author as mere
pot-boilers, unworthy of publication. One of his
hearers, however, was Thomas Chisholm Anstey.
He reported them for his own use, and it is from
Anstey’s MSS. that the present book is printed.
Young Anstey was a son of Thomas Anstey, J.P.,
of Anstey-Barton, one of the famous Midland
estates in Tasmania (which figures in this week’s
story by “Price Waning”), now in the possession
of the Pages. His early education was communi-
cated partly by William Thomson, afterwards the
noted cattle-breeder of Keilambete, Western
Victoria, but he had for his French master no
less a person than Jorgen Jorgenson, the ex-
traordinary character of whom Marcus Clarke
wrote: —
All the raven-haired, hot-headed, supple-wristed soldiers
of fortune that ever diced, drank, duelled, kissed and
escaladed their way through three volumes octavo, never
had such an experience.
Jorgenson was for ten weeks Governor of Iceland,
having deposed the Danish Governor. He was
removed from power as unceremoniously as he had
assumed it by the commander of a British sloop-
of-war, and, after a number of vicissitudes,, was
transported to Van Demonia for forgery—which
was not his first visit to the island, as he had been
present at the founding of the colony. For a time
he was magistrate’s clerk to Mr. Anstey, and
coached young Anstey in French. The young
fellow went, in due course, to an English Uni-
versity, to the Bar, and into Parliament for an
Irish seat. He was the first native of an Austra-
lian colony to sit in the House of Commons. For
appropriate services to Government he was
pitchforked into an Indian judgeship, whereby he
by no means distinguished himself. Duffy has a
lot to say about him in one of his Young Ireland
histories, but Duffy did not know that Anstey had
been rudimentarily educated by a convict, for he
could not have resisted the temptation to cynically
allude to it. Jorgenson, by the way, figures pro-
minentlv in Hall Caine’s story, “ The Bondman ”
—but the drawing of the story, while powerful, is
distinguished by the amplest departure from fact.

Sydney, asks the Postmaster-General to stop the

his thumb against his nose and spreads his fingers
out,’ with tho remark —“ Why, them ’ere bells
c >st £oon.~’ This tangential answer is met by
0 h t objectors with the argument that, if the

r J li 3 letter in S.M. Herald, denouncing
the proposed release of the murderer Bertrand, is
signed “S.” —with a view, perhaps, to its being
rttrdmtel to Sir Alfred Stephen, who should
Iro nptly deny its authorship. “ S.” rtfgtles that
the Mount Ronnie affair, “ for which we hanged
several youths,” did not compare in atrocity with
Bertrands. Which is a poor argument, con-
s dering that the Mount Rennie youths were not
tried but lynched, not executed but assassinated,
and that there is every reason to believe that, at
least, one of them had no share whatever in the
transaction. The Bulletin is in favour of the
abolition of capital punishment, and so would
keep such a man as Bertrand in gaol literally for
life. However, there is no earthly reason why
Bertrand should bo made an exception to the
general practice of releasing a prisoner who has
served the actuarial term of his life-expectancy,
simply because he has offended specially against
certain influential members of high society.

A fellow-passenger with “Albert Wil-
liams ” on the Kaiser Wilhelm 11. tells a Bulle-
tin correspondent that “ Williams ” made himself
extremely popular with the ladies on board. He
fairly oozed with respectful attentions to the fair
sex, for whom he retrievered all round in the
finest fetoh-and-carry style. Some instances A
child of one of the passengers one day came on
deck shod in indiarubber-bottomed canvas shoes,
the soles of which were loose. “ Williams” volun-
teered to mend the bottines, took them down to
his cabin, and, in a few minutes, returned with
them as good as new- thoroughly “ cemented,” in
fact. “ Williams’s ” cabin was a regular workshop,
always remarkable for littered chips and multi-
farious odds - and - ends. One of his favourite
pastimes was to carve out toy boat-models for
children, with whom he was as popular as with
their mothers and aunts. One interesting little
boy used to haunt the man’s cabin, with his tiny
arm hugging affectionately “Williams’s” waist,
while “Williams” was executing for him the
model of a ship. “ Williams ” promised to visit
that lad in Sydney and there make for him a
model of a locomotive. -Now, the little man is
continually asking his parents—“ When is Mr.
Williams coming to see us and make me an
injin?” When, indeed?

A respectable female, who was Deeming’s
landlady at one time, ,now comes to the front to
state that she was always of opinion that ho was
“haunted.” She didn’t mention it to anybody at
the time, but she distinctly remembers now that
the idea occurred to her. Also, the sister of Mbs
Rounsevelle positively recollects that she was sus-
picions about him, only apparently she forgot to
say so till the other day." At the same time, she
wrote gushing letters to the baleful lover, and told
him that she regarded him “as a brother.” It
was ever thus. If it transpired—which, of
course, it couldn't-that that pleasant pun-
ster, of the beaming face, Mr. Zox, had
murdered somebody when he was young,
upwards of sixty people would rue up at once to
swear that he always had a haggard, hunted look
in his eyes, that he would shriek if left alone in
the dark, that ho saw ghosts, and moaned in his
sleep, and used to babble incoherent words about
skulls and dead men’s bones ; also, that sometimes,
when he was engaged in conversation, he would
suddenly grow pale and his knee? would bang
together, and then he would utter a piercing
scream and run away. And then it would pro-
bably turn out that it wasn’t Zox who had com-
mitted the crime, and everybody would straightway
retract all these statements again.

Snake-story told by Dr. J. C. Cox, F.L.S.,
of Sydney. A brother scientist had fallen upon
alcoholic times, and to keep him straight the
veteran naturalist despatched him on a collecting
tour. Suspecting that the evaporation which
always occurred in the spirit-drum was not wholly
due to natural causes, he slipped, aceidentally-
like, a b’ack snake into the next consignment.
The following mail brought a letter from the
prodigal. “ Your case,” said he, “came safe to
hand, and yon will be glad to hear that the
hoplocephalns porphyreticus much improved tho
flavour.”

There was a general public impression
that the late quiet-going Sir John Hay, President
of N.S.W. Legislative Council, was a very rich
and a very “near” man. He was certainly never
ostentatious in his liberality, but an examination
into his estate made by his executors affords a
striking illustration of how very little the public
often know of the real character of some of its
most prominent public men. Sir John Hay was
so sensitively secretive that even his wife was not
fully aware of the open-handed manner in which
he privately spent his money in deeds that were
as modest as they were healthfully philanthropic.
The old man never kept any private accounts,
save his cheque and bank-books, and from these
it has now become apparent that he gave away
nearly the whole of his income every year. In
his personal habits he was so simple that the ex-
penses of his own establishment came easily
within his income as President of the N.S.W.
Legislative Council, although ho kept open house
for a largo number of friends and his continued
hospitality was chiefly founded upon a delicate
and kindly benevolence, lie was a most liberal—
but often unknown—patron of the scientific and
charitable institutions of N.S.W., and his name
was seldom absent from any wholesome subscrip-
tion-list, besides appearing in others concerning
which there were sectional differences of opinion,
lie could have profitably quitted his large interest
in the Mercantile Bank of Sydney, and avoided
all chance of loss by unloading to the public, but,
though the bank’s practical collapse cost him
many thousands of pounds sterling, he never sold
a single share. But he did even more than stand
by the ruin. Several ladies had taken shares in
that bank chiefly on his recommendation, and he
had such a rarely-seen sense of honour that, as a
matter of duty, he insisted on paying these
ladies the full extent of their losses.

Mr. Maxfced, N.S.W. Inspector of Chari-
ties, is a humourist. He writes :
At the same time 1 may mention that I have already
brought under the notice of the board of the asylum the
unreliableness of statements made by women concerning
the paternity of their children.
There's an original revelation for you ! However,
to do Mr. Maxtor! justice, that is not the only
thing ha has found out. For instance, he lets the
public know that there is in Sydney a private
lying-in home where 90 illegitimate births took
place in the one year, only seven of which were
officially recorded. In another private lying-in
home where GO or 70 illegitimate births occurred
during the year, the matron told him she thought
it quite unlikely that any of the infants were
registered, as the bulk of the women came to her
under assumed names. Then Mr. Maxted in his
travels met one “ woman in the habit of receiving
infants for adoption for <£s. and taking them
round to unsuspecting persons, promising payment
of 7s. Gd. a week, and then disappearing.”

The affairs in insolvency of “ Hanker ”
Friedman, of Sydney, are now commencing to
assume a low-comedy aspect. Friedman’s adopted
nephew or something like that, who arrived in
N.S. Wales about eight years ago, but who
doesn’t know how old he was when he came; who
might then have been 10 years old, or might have
been 15; who knew no English, and when he
commenced school started in the infants’ branch ;
who had £l5O on arrival, which he “ always kept
about Ills person,” and who since then had lent
his uncle £2300 in all; who speculated in shares,
but doesn’t know what shares, and who won £3OO
in “TattersalTa Sweep;” and who has been
debited in Friedman's books with a lot of money
recorded over erasures —well, the world yearns
to hear more about this noble youth.

“BREAKING IT GENTLY.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
All was up with Richard Tanner—
"Wait-a-Bit” we called him. Dead?
Yes. The braceman dropped a spanner.
Landed Richard on the head ;
Cracked his skull, sir, like a lea-cup,
Down the pump-shaft in tbe well
Braceman hadn’t time to speak up.
Tanner never knew what fell.
When we fetched him to the surface,
Dick had struck, and got a rise—
See how dark that patch of turf is !
There he bled before our eyes.
And we stood around him, gory
I.ike himself, and just as pale,
Wondering who would tell the story,
Who would tell his wife the tale.
No one liked to undertake it,
No one on the shift would stir ;
But Pat Ryan said he’d break it
‘‘Nately break the news to her.”
Pat’s a splitter, and a kinder
Heart I never want to know.
Stephens told him where to find her,
Begged him gently deal the blow.
In a very solemn manner
Ryan met the dead man’s wife—
“ Homin’ to yez, Widdy Tanner !”
Says he gravely. “ Such is life !”
“ ]'m no widow !” says she, prying
.For the joke in Ryan’s eye.
“’Sense me, mum,” says Paddy, crying,
“’Sense me, mum, hut that's a lie.”
E. D.

f ray, sec yourselves with English eyes

A side-issue of the discovery of Deeming
is the unearthing of a bigamous official living in
comparative seclusion. His name chanced to be
published in connection with certain legal pro-
ceedings ; this discovered him to a wife who has
long looked for him. Now in the hands of the
Sydney detectives are his photograph and copies
of his first and second marriage-certificates.

The hostile Sydney Chinese factions are
arming themselves —with Colt’s repeating-rifles.
As there are about 16,000 Chinese too many in
N.S. Wales, The Bulletin proposes that the
Government lend them the Centennial Park for a
week or two to “ have it out.” The wild beasts of
the Zoo. are at hand to oat up the dead, and the
tram which runs hard by can shunt the living,
leprous balance right up to Darlinghurst prison
pending their exportation per first charterable
steamers.

been scandalised lately by a well-known Israelite

of the Chosen the lady signified her willingness to
become a Jewess and was duly “received.” It
was then discovered that the bridegroom had com-
mitted a grievous breach of law by wedding a lady
who wasn’t a Jewess beforehand. For this both
the bridegroom and bride have been expelled the

A Nowra, N.S.W.. farmer has a family of
23, mostly boys. The tribe is celibate through-
out and works on the farm of its founder, who is
so constant a Bible-reader that he almost knows
the Scriptures by heart. “Blessed is the man
that hath his quiver full of them” —for they shall
abide by him for fear of following his example.
* * *
Regarded as a really business-like female,
a'certain Mrs. Amelia Johnston, of Maoriland,
seems to take a high place. According to her own
statement, she was married, in 1878 or 1879, to
one Sutherland, who deserted her a couple of
years later. In 1882 she got an order against him
for maintenance for herself and her two children.
Then she started a divorce-suit against Suther-
land, and next she heard that he was dead.
Thereupon she married a person by the name of
Johnston, and, by-and-bye, he died also. After
lie was dead she learned to her surprise that
Sutherland was still alive, and now she is suing
him for £213 10s., being alimony for the whole
time that she was living with Johnston. This
arrangement scorns to be very pleasant for all
parties with the exception of Johnston. If that
unlucky person were alive, what a time he could
have out of the alimony !

deck with flakes of granite sacred to the memory
of John Smith.

« # *
The gentleman who defended Pat. Cleary
(of South Melbourne fame) did his best. After a
bank-manager witness for the Crown had given
some formal evidence re cheques, he was cross-
examined by Patrick’s champion. “How long
has Cleary had a private account at your bank?”
“For the last two or three years,” replied the
witness. “And what sort of a person have you
found him?” “Nothing different to any other
ordinary sort of a customer,” was the answer.
Whereupon thecross-examiner looked triumphantly
at the jury, as though the. fact that Cleary hadn’t
attempted to sneak the till were a strong point in
his favour. It is about time that somebody wrote
a poem on the puerilities of the law-courts.
# * *
A Melbourne paper states that Mrs.
Bromby, the juvenile widow of the venerable
Rev. Dr. Bromby, is engaged to a Mr. Hunt. It
will be remembered that Bishop Moorhouse made
himself ridiculous in trying to quash the Bromby
wedding, and smugly fancied he bad done so,
until the news of the Queensdiff ceremony was
sprung upon him. There was a screamingly funny
scene in the Church Assembly while Bromby, just
on the eve of the affair, was urging a reduction
of marriage fees; the reporters had to half-swallow
their hats, while the reverends were as grim as
death.

# # #
One of the big Southern coffee-palaces has
hired a new manager who is obviously resolved to
make things hum. Judging by his methods an
impression is getting abroad that he must have
been a bad-debt collector in his early life. It is
the rule of the establishment that all payments
must be made in advance, and the other morning,
about two a.in., it was discovered that a lady and
her daughter, then in bed upstairs, had failed to
settle up. They had been living in the establish-
ment for several months, and had always paid
monthly in advance, but this time they were
twelve hours overdue. Straightway the porter
clumped heavily upstairs, and issued an
ultimatum through the keyhole; the money was
wanted right there, and the ladies were desired to
get out of bed and square up. They declined to
get out of bed—and the porter promptly ran for a
policeman. Then, at 2.30 a.m., in a fashionable
caravanserai, did the bulky minion of the law
walk ponderously up the stair, and demand in
hoarse accents that the two ladies should get out
of that. The story is unfortunately an unfinished
one, and whether the ladies got rut of that, or
whether they shoved the three shillings under-
neath the door, is not ascertained. But business
keeps steadily falling-off in the coffee-palaces.
* o #
Miss Dalrymple, a professor of palmistry
and astrology, now all the rage in London, rays
that small-thumbed people are not worth very
much, and that strength and excellence of
character increase with the length of this digit.
Those about to marry are advised to inspret the
little fingers of their fiancees, as whichever of the
married couple possessed the longer fourth-finger
would be the ruler in the matrimonial struggle for
supremacy.

Mrs. Parnell has squared her £'12,000 law-
suit by sharing equally with her opponents the
property involved.
* * *
There is secret rejoicing in the hearts of
many Tasmanians over the sudden cb'sappearance
of the girl Cope, who is badly wanted to prove
the charge of malpractice preferred against the
popular Doctor Bing. Crowther. Neither is Miss
Cope’s alleged friend Ritchie, the young solicitor,
to be found. Meanwhile, the Doctor comes up
weekly on remand, and the case will probably end
in a fizzle.
* * #
The management of one of the Australian
banks is, it is said, gravely considering the
advisability of deducting ten per cent, of their
clerks’ salaries, to form a fund from wh’ch to re-
imburse themselves for future losses by embezzle-
ment.
# # #
Cabled that the post-mortem on Schneider,
executed at Vienna for atrocious murders, revealtd
that the convict had been suffering from brain-
dropsy. In other words, that Schneider was more
than prima facie insane. “They do these things
better in Australia.” When they hang a man
whom the doctors suspect is insane, .they refuse
(as in the case of Colston) to allow any post-mortem
to be made. It saves scandal.
A SONG FROM A SANDHILL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Drip, drip, drip ! It tinkles on the “ fly ” —
The pitiless outpouring of an overburdened sky.
Every drooping frond of pine has got a jewel at its tip—
First a twinkle, then a sprinkle, and a drip, drip, drip.
Drip, drip, drip ! They must be shearing up on High,
Can’t you see the snowy fleeces that are rolling, rolling
by?
How many hales, I wonder,arc they branding to the clip?
P’raps the Boss is keeping tally with this drip, drip, drip.
Drip, drip, drip ! while the sodden branches sigh,
The jovial jackass dare not laugh for fear that he should
cry ;
The merry magpie’s melody is frozen on his lip,
He glowers at the showers, with their drip, drip, drip
Drip, drip, drip ! and one’s “ nap ” is far from dry,
Tis hard to keep the water out - however one may try;
I’d sell myself to Satan tor three fingers of a nip,
There’s cramps and rheumatism in that drip, drip, drip,
Pat, pat, pat! how it patters on the land !
’Tis certainly consoling to hi camped upon the sand,
There’s naught hut mud and water over yonder on the
flat,
Where the spots of rain arc splashing with their pat, pat,
pat.
Rain, rain, rain ! and the day is nearly done,
I wonder shall we see another rising of the son?
lias the sky shut down and stifled him, or will he come
again
And stop the cursed clatter of this rain, rain, rain?
Drop, drop, drop ! monotonous as Lite,
With now and then a western breeze that cuts one as a
knife ;
Sputter on the fire, is it never going to stop?
lias the weather-clerk gone crazy, with his drop, drop,
drop.
Drip, drip, drip ! the squatter wouldn't say
“Thank God!” so earnestly if he were camped in it
to-day;
Tis in at last—I knew it—there’s a pool about my hip—
Oh, ’tis maddening ami sad’ning, with its drip, drip, drip.

THE WINDSOR MURDERER.
Avaunt! Thou damn6d, cursed Fiend of Hell!
E’en the brute-beasts, melhinks, snort out thy kncl>
Of death !—revolting from thy deeds cf crime 1
For, over since the world can count its time,
The brute-beast didst love its young. But thou— thou,
To whom dear woman’s nature must e’en how
When once thy serpent tongue let loose—didst lure
Wife—wives-God help them !—doubtless all were pure
’Till thy foul breath—till thy vile tongue (fiend-ilk.)
Did gentle hearts—their common senses strike
With blindfold love !
Love for what ? Love for whom ?
A fiend of Hell! Aye—doubt not- Hell’s thy doom !
To think that thou hadst mix’d ’mong maidens pure.
The very thought is sick’ning to endure.
But woman’s curse—and maidens’ pray’r will be —
Fierce, piercing torments— thy eternity !
Yea ! The brute-beasts protect their living young 1
From age to age, brute-nature 1ms been sung
Showing how brute-beasts fight for their cub’s life—
But thou—demon ! —must kill both child and «ife.
By the score, no doubt! Their sad muster-roll
Will add ten-thousand curses to thy soul !
The nation—ave the world at large will curs',
And thankful be -thou cahst not ride in hearse.
The gibbet be thy death ! In gibbet chains
To hang ! Blister !
When not a bone rem i ns -
Thy record still—
Through crime of crimes will he,
Champion Murd’rcr!
Avaunt ! To Hell with thee !

THE MELBOURNIAN TOAST.
Come, fill your glass brimming high
And drink, my hearties !
Confound all politics, say I,
And petty parties.
Here's one great universal toas'
The Loard preserve our penny post 1
Just toss it off with three times three,
My noble welters,
And make the welkin ring, for wo
Are men of letters.
Though country members rule the roast
We’ll rally round our penny post.
’Tis true the farmer builds the town
And feeds the city,
’Tis also true his face is brown
And sometimes gritty ;
But we, the pale commercial host,
Have given him a penny post.
The tax to him appeareth light,
On us ’twere heavy,
The more a wrong the more you wii.te—
This extra levy.
And we who feel depression most
Cry—“ Board preserve our penny post !”


Says Sydney E. News :
There is some modern reading that, at least for the
ignorant or semi-ignorant, must be as debasing as witness-
ing executions.
Heaven alone knows why Alfred Bennett let
this go into the Nooze.
Omitted from the newspaper accounts :
Evidence is to hand that “Williams the Mur-
derer” was once in Moomboomberrinjigobung,
No-man’s-land. Tracks have been discovered
there on the surface of a perennial lagoon present-
ing characteristics that reveal that the imprinter
was a fair-thatched, heavy-jowled, short, broad
man, who had led the leaden life of a plumber,
was much married, and had fled from his landlady
without settling up his board-bill. The lagoon is
to be drained off, in the expectation of discovering
some carefully-drowned hearthstones. The pecu-
liar ripple of the lagoon in the vicinity of the
tracks wears an ominously murderous smile, and
the water is decidedly alkaline, thougli blood-red.
The natives call the lagoon “Mamibung”—liter-
ally, “The Golgotha of the Creamy Bunyip !”

Rosebery’s “ Pitt ” on the celebrated Irish
Viceroy :
FUzwilliam appears to have thought that Ireland was
made over to him, as were Lampsacus and Magnesia to
Themistoeles for his bread and wine.
But Fitzwiiliam was a man of generous sympathies
and honest enthusiasm; and his fall as Viceroy
was due to his encouragement of the Catholics.
He was the patron of D’Arcy Wentworth, father
of W. C. Wentworth, and the latter’s son, the
well-known Australian sportsman, Fitzwiiliam
Wentworth, was named after the Earl.

QUEENSLAND.
NOTICE.
Sale by Auction oe Crown Lam s in Large
Portions.
IT is notified for public information that, under
the provisions of “ The Special Sales of Land
Act of 1891,” Country Lands in the Colony of
Queensland, distant not less than twenty miles
from a navigable river, or from a railway, or from
the surveyed line of a railway for the construction
of which money has been raised by loan, may be
offered for Sale bv Auction, in portions not exceed-
ing 5120 acres. The upset price will in no case be
less than 10s. per acre.
The time for payment may be extended to a
period not exceeding three years, instalments
payable later than six months from the date of
sale bearing interest at 5 per cent, per annum.
Persons desirous of having Crown lands offered
ab auction are invited to apply to the Under
Secretary for Public Lands, Brisbane, or any
Crown Lands Commissioner.
A. S. COWLEY,
Secretary for Public Lands.
Department of Public Lands,
Brisbane, 28th October, 1891.
E A SICKNESS.
There are few things that take the conceit out
of a man more surely and speedily than sea sick-
ness. It's all very well to sing about “ A life on
the ocean wave.” When you get there you feel
different about it. The movements of the vessel
become much too previous for an erect attitude to
be maintained with dignity, and you experience a
glorious uncertainty as to whether you displayed
wisdom in dining with a few friends before coming
on board. That very act of injudiciousness on
your part is probably the cause of nearly all the
trouble. The greatest care should be taken by
those who have occasion to brave “the sea, the
sea, the open sea’’that indulgence of every kind
is carefully avoided for some days prior to embark-
ment. Let the stomach and liver be in a normal,
healthy state, and there is not much cause for
apprehension, A hearty meal of stale bread or
biscuits and well matured cheese (the more eaten
the better), washed down with a pint of the best
bottled stout, should be taken soon after going on
board. This will induce sleep, for which purpose
let the passenger retire to his or her berth and
remain there for several hours. By that time all
unpleasant symptoms will probably have dis-
appeared.
If not, let him reach for his portmanteau, and it
this has been packed by a person possessed of any
fore-thought it will be found to contain a bottle of
St. Jacobs Oil. A contemplation of the amount
of human misery which the use of that famous
remedy has been instrumental in putting to flight
will inspire cheerful thoughts, and cheerfulness
(as is well known) “ doeth good like a medicine.”
Former rheumatic and neuralgic patients every-
where testify that the most pleasant experience of
their lives is their acquaintance with so invaluable
a friend as the above pain-conquering preparation.

LUXENE,
PROOF AGAINST EXPLOSION,
OLITE LYING.
When people lay themselves out to discover
reasons for not doing things they can always find
them. Some people are wonderfully proficient at
the business, and can rake up apparently valid
excuses from the most unpromising material. We
all know the man who wriggles out of the disgrace
of a broken appointment on the pretence that he
was seized by sudden indisposition. Then there
is the individual who excuses his close-fistedness
on the plea that indiscriminate charity has some-
times led to grave abuses, and he fears to be party
to a wrong. The crowning point, however, in the
deceptive, apologetic art lias been readied by a
couple of professional boxers, who seriously
announce that a slugging match arranged between
them has been postponed on account of the death
of an old friend abroad seven weeks previously.
These people are just as likely to be believed as
the man who, by way of excuse for his irritating
habit of constantly inquiring the time of the day,
exclaims that he “can't afford to carry a watch.”
An excuse such as this is a very lame one indeed,
seeing that a thoroughly reliable Waterbury
Watch, in either nickel, silver or gold, may be
obtained of respectable jewellers and dealers
almost everywhere at a figure so nominal that the
humblest may possess a reliable timekeeper. We
have had the opportunity to inspect the new series
“ N ” (ladies’) and series “K|” (gentlemen’s) short-
wind, stem-set Waterburys, both in rolled gold
and silver cases. They are at once elegant in
appearance, strong, durable, inexpensive, and
easy of adjustment and repair, the whole of the
parts being absolutely interchangeable.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Two lady sloggers, Hattie Leslie, “the female
John B. Sullivan,” and Gnssie Freeman, professionally
known as “ Lonely,” undertook to fight to a finish recently
tit Brooklyn (U.S.). The female johnel was dressed in
black fighting tights and a white silk tunic, and the other
. enlle creature appeared in pure white. They fought
geient finally enough for three rounds, and then Gussie
got her golden head in chancery, and lost her temper, and
the'rust of the round consisted of frantic clawing, biting,
Kicking, screeching, and tearing of hair and tights. The
combatants were torn apart just in time to prevent them
biting e ic!i other to death, and were led a" ay, each
s taking her list at the other, and casting reflections on
the tit of her bonnet. Women never will shine in the
prize-fighting business. When one of them is knocked
down, she always will try to get square while she is sitting
on the floor, by telling the other one that her new frock
looks just like a bag, and then the other one gets enraged
and hits her when she is down.
* • *
Dear Bulletin, —None of the stories told of
leviathan bookmaker Joe Thompson’s voyage to Hing-
land seem to touch upon the fact that he attached himself
quite religiously to an elderly Melbourne parson whom he
always addressed as “ reverend sir.” Out of respect for
the cloth Joseph made a point of cutting short his excur-
sions into swear language with “Beg pardon! reverend
sir,” and so, on the whole, the “ book ” and the “ rook ”
got on very well together. One day, indeed, Joseph in-
duced his friend to come down to the bar and have a
drink. “What will you take, reverend sir? ” asked the
proud leviathan. Tiie parson decided in favour of ginger-
ale. “ ll 'hat! that rot-gut stuff,” shrieks Joseph,
forgetting himself, as usual. Then lie begged pardon
with extra humility, and, gazing sadly at the steward,
ordered ginger-ale for the reverend sir. That trip
“ ’ome ” was a healthy tonic for Joseph’s soul, if he has a
soul. —One Who Was There at the Time.

The original Spencer, of Spencer Bros., the
well-known Sydney rowing-patrons, seems quite unable
ever to mention Searle byname. “The one that’s dead
and Sullivan are the only scientific scullers I have
known.”

That plucky Sydney youngster, Helli ngs, lowered
the SDO yards amateur swimming record by 2 2-5 seconds,

# # *
Will Queensland win the eight-oar race this
year ? Last year’s crew was really the Breakfast Creek
crew, and their victory was a bitter knock to the rival
shed —the Commercial which advocates an entirely
different style of rowing.
» * •
In his recent letter to the Sydney cricket
Association, re the disputed umpire’s decision, W. G.
Grace said that the members of his team were unanimous
in asserting that Lohmann, when delivering the ball to
Charlton, did not get in front of umpire Briscoe. The
only men in a position to judge would be the wicket-
keeper and straight-hit, because the sole way to decide
whether three points are in a straight line is by placing
yourself behind one of the extreme points. And there’s
no getting behind this mathematical truth.
* * #
In one Sydney oyster-saloon from four to a
dozen pipes are left per week and never called for.
Oysters soothe the soul even of the smoker, and make
him forgetful of himself.
• # *
The English team, invited by the Mayor of
Adelaide to meet the Australians in a formal welcome,
did not in any way recognise the invitation. Grace again.
He is reported to have said: “No grog till we lick’em.
The side which wins the last match is the winning side, all
over the earth.”
» • »
George Lohmann, whose father is a clergyman,
has been captured by a Melbourne girl. She goes to
England with him.
The final test-match, England v. Australia, has
been won by the weather. A curious coincidence: In the
second match, Lyons scored 134 ; Stoddart scored 134 in
this match. The'record now is-Australia, two matches ;
weather, one.
• • #
Down the Parramatta River, last Sunday, Stan-
bury and Sullivan “showed” for a couple of miles in
front of their steamer. The ohanqiions sculling side by
side—Stanbury, a swarthy giant ; Sullivan, younger,
softer, and quite white —looked a physical Esau and
Jacob. The crowd generally backs the strong man, and
we are with the crowd this time.
• • *
Sweeps in Australia are doomed, provided the
result of the Australasian Postal Conference is to make
uniform the method of dealing with correspondence
relating to consultations and gambling bets.
# * #
At a dinner given to bookmaker Sam. Allen
(Melb.), where everyone present was either a Mo., Ike, or
Sol., “three hearty Australian cheers were given for
Queen Victoria.” Also, the diners sang “a few bars of
the ‘ National Anthem.’ ” S’elp us ! They were probably
thinking of the Grand National.
# * »
The Griffo-Mick M'Carthy fight at Sydney
Olynqiic diew a big crowd. Mick Mao. is a brother to
Billy Mac. Both men were a shade under 9st., but while
Griffo looked what he is—a perfect pocket Tom Sayers—
M’Carthy looked “poor.” The latter has never at any
time shown himself a first-flight boxer. His struggle with
Griffo did not denote any improvement, as his opponent
simply hit him when and where he liked, and put him
into his little cot at the end of the fourth round.
M’Carthy was much disfigured.
# # #
Apropos a certain Melbourne burglary, people
are saying—“As you sow so shall you reap.”
« • •
In a back number of London Athen.eum, one
turns up a flattering notice of the poetry volume called
“Sparks and Sounds from a Colonial Anvil,” by John
Whiteman, Madame Midas’ husband, and wonders why he
left off writing poetry. A queer thing, though, was his
re-publishing his metrical Melbourne Cup tips, ail altered
to the name of the winning horse.
m * *
There is no attempt at consistency in the police
attitude towards betting. At Richmond (Melb.) pony
races, a would-be welsher was captured by a few fleet-
footed backers and handed over to a constable, who con-
ducted the slippery bagman back to the ring, and kept
watch over him until he had duly squared accounts. Off
the course the same man might be summoned by the same
limb of the law for plying his trade in a public place. The
police seemingly recognise offences against one party in a
proceeding which is equally illegal on both sides.
• # •
The other day a Sydney parson visited the bed-
side of a “ dying ” stable-boy. “ I’m afraid you can’t get
better, my little man,” said the good pastor. “Betyer
ten ter one I can, sir,” answered the lad. And he did.

the moral slate of N.S. Wales wants this printed;—
Where drink and ignorance reign, contentment fails.
And honour sinks where gambling long prevails.
* # *
For those who want to know how many com-
binations are derivable from various groups of figures,
the following is a simple and workable method. Take
three words—as an example “ cash on delivery” : here six
combinations are possible. To prove this the enquirer
adds up first the total number of words in the sentence,
i.e., three. Then he arranges a system of numerals to
descend from tills ascertained total until the unit 1 is
reached, and multiplies them all together, the result
giving the answer thus: 3x2xl-6. In a combination of
17 words the enquirer would see that 17xlCxl5xl4xl3x
12x11x10x9x8x7x6x5x4x3x2x1 the variations possible
would reach 355,687,428,096,000 ! This may afford an
idea of the odds against the Chinese lottery speculator.

rence, has just retired from the N.S.W. railway-service, in

# # *
Mr. gam. Hordern’s luck sticks to him on land
and sea. His imported two-and a-half-ton yacht, Bronze-
wing, has just put down Mr. J. O. Fairfax’s crack, Bul-
bul, in two consecutive races.
# * #
Miss Mayo, of the Italian Opera Co., drowned
on Saturday night last, owes her death to the fact that
she went out in a boat manned by deep sea sailors instead
of Port Jackson amateurs. Professional sailors can ride
horses even better than they can handle a boat in a
breeze.

The recent unsteadiness of a great Australian
cricketer is all along of a fickle girl.

A concise report of the third test match between
the Englishmen and All Australia, at Adelaide: The
Englishmen, on a perfect wicket, in Ist innings made 499.
Australia, witli rain and bad wicket, Ist innings, 100 ; 2nd
innings, worse wicket, 169—ns in nine matches mil of ten
between anything like equal teams, the toss decided the
match, and no true test match can be played until some
genius invents a gutta-percha pitch of the same pace as
the best turf.
* * *
Bungebah, at 7 to 1, won the Rosehill Cup, all
out, by a head from Helene. Helene was a trifle on the
big side, and her running on Saturday makes her chance
for the Doncaster look rosy, for she meets Bungebah on
81b. better terms; and as there is authority for stating
that Paris is lame—how lame is not known—Helene’s
show should be second to none.
Cannon, Mr. Sam. Hordern’s colt by Norden-
feldt, is in appearance a magnificent animal. He started
in the Nursery Handicap at Rosehill on Saturday, and,
getting away very badly, finished fourth.

Cable item. More “ submerged tenth
London, Tuesday.—ln the House of Commons last night
Mr. Hugh Watt, the Gladstone Liberal member for Cam-
lachie (Glasgow), asked whether the Government would,
favour the transfer of the congested populations of Eng-
land and India to the Northern Territory in South Aus-
tralia?
“ Congested population ” sounds good, especially
when we read on the same day that, in the tiny
kingdom of Scotland alone, and only within the
last eight years, 244,440 acres have been cleared of
their population—the said populationbeing “con-
gested ” into the big cities —and turned into deer-
forest, in order that the rich and fatted Smith, of
London, may make a fool of himself on a damp
hillside with a gun.
Of “ Black and Crick” the world is sick.
How is it that so many general managers
of Australian banks are going to England now ?
Out of 450 Chinamen imported to New
Guinea by the German Company in October last,
300 had, up to February 2Gth., died of fever,
dysentery, &c.
The Brisbane Worker will appear as a
weekly this Saturday. W. Lane, editor; Frank
Barnes, recently of Young Australia, Sydney,
business manager.
Cheerful items from London: The Stan-
hard predicts that silver, which at latest was
3s. 4 7-l Gd. peroz., will fall to 2s. Gd. London
wool-buyers fear that wool, excepting crossbred
and pure merino, will fall a half-penny.
At Croydon, (Q.) Cingalese, Malays, &c.,
take in washing, A lot of them make aboriginal
gins do all the hard work while they merely iron
and take round the boiled shirt to collect the cash.
This is playing it pretty low down on our country-
women.
In spite of the promise of Sayers, M.L.A-
(Q.), to vote against Kanakas and to support a
no-confidence motion (if proposed) against the
Griffilwraith Ministry, the Associated Workers
of Charters Towers have officially requested both
him and his colleague, ex-parson and now-lawyer
Rutledge, to resign.
According to Detective Cawsey, Deeming’s
fits are artful imitations designed to bolster up the
insanity theory. Apropos , another man with a
fractured skull has been picked up in Sydney and
run into a watchhouse cell for “ drunkenness ;’ r
found still unconscious many hours afterwards he
was when too late taken to the hospital. Police-
men arc failures as diagnosticians.
“Our beautiful harbor” has to be bracketed
with “Our beautiful hotels,” and it is a curious
coincidence that sea-caterers frequently turn out
the best land-caterers, probably owing to the
sumptuous vogue of travel now-a-days. Mr. •).
Scott-Black, proprietor of the George Hotel, Pitt
and Market Streets, is no exception fo this rule.
For six years, chief steward in the E. & A. Co.’s
China trade, and with a record also embracing
periods in the P.M.S.N. Co.’s Frisco service and
the A.U.S.N. Co.’s, in the latter of which he was
Chief and Inspecting-Steward, Mr. Black is well
qualified to manage a first-class hotel. The
George is centrally situated in the heart of the
city, and yet in close proximity to the parks and
places of amusement, and within the easiest of
communication with all the delightful surround-
ings of Sydney. The hotel can accommodate 50
guests, and has just undergone complete renova-
tion and refurnishment. It possesses a fine library
and reading-room, a luxurious drawing-room, a
centrally placed commercial room, private suites,
&c., &c. The corner hitherto rented by the Aus-
tralian Banking Co. has been included in the
hotel proper, and has been furnished with luxurious
modern appointments to do service as two private
bars and a writing-room for the convenience of com-
mercial and business guests. Hot and cold water
baths, and spacious lavatory accommodation are
Xirovided on every floor, to which access is gained by
a comfortable elevator, and the individual comteit
of every guest is made a matter of special study.
The cuisine and the cellar may be relied upon;
Mr. Black is caterer to the Municipal Council —
and Alderman are reputed to understand the art
of bodily refreshment.

Cardinal Manning, several years ago, to his friend,

“Nothing could be sadder than the death
of John Multy, through a tree falling on him,”
says a Maoriland paper, in its obituary upon a
squashed resident. Well, supposing two trees
had fallen on him ?
The judicial murder of three poachers, for
slaying two English gamekeepers-in a poaching-
fray, will be a big card in the hands of the British
Radical party at next general election. May the
horror and rage of the democracy at the
cold-blooded vengeance thus exacted by the
governing classes be the means of dynamiting the
game laws !

Government analyst Hamlet can, in the
main, find only harmless adulterations in the
N.S.W. liquor. It is true that one sample of
beer, a glass of which made the drinker seriously
ill, was found to contain strychnine in the propor-
tion of 0.34 gr. to the pint. A quart of such
liquor would mean death to anybody.
Use Batho’s Baking Powder.
Attorney Sam Lyons (Melb.) referred The
other day to the exceptional severity of the sen-
tences passed by Justice Williams. Someyeais
ago the Judge rendered himself ludicrous by
letting off Sieber, the Hungarian would-be bar-
maid-shooter, with a lecture on the mischief of
reading Kotzebue, and having been roasted over
that, Williams now lets off steam upon the
wretched prisoners. All which again points the
moral of the jury passing the sentehce, as in the
United States. Why should a sentence vary
from six weeks to six years, according to one man’s
caprice ?

A Way to Make a Living.
“ Fill in a proposal to the Universal Mutual
Benefit and Life Assurance Co., Unlimited, Bob.
I’m canvassing for them now, on half-a-sov. a
week and commission.”
“What’s the use? I can’t afford to pay even
the first premium.”
“ Oh, you’ve no noyii to pay anything. I’ll give
you a receipt for Su. Od. That will run you for a
quarter. I’ll get <£l commission, and so I stand
to win 10s. 3d. on the transaction.”
“Is that the trick? All right; you fill in the
form and I’ll sign it. But what about the medical
examination ?”
“You won’t have to undergo any medical
examination for so small an amount as that, and
it will assure you a decent funeral should you die
while assured. After the quarter, you can keep
on paying if you like.”
“But I won’t like. 11l never pay anything,
and the policy will lapse.”
“ All serene. But 111 make 10s. 3d., and we’ll
have a liquor or two on the proceeds, fiign your
name there, my boy.”
“ Do you do much in this business?”
“ Rather. Most of the knowing ones do it.
Why, we couldn’t live on the game, if we didn’t.”
“But, at the end of the quarter, how do you
stand with the office;? Don’t you get the sack?”
“ They don’t get the chance to sack me. About
the eleventh week of my first lapser falling due, 1
drop the contract. Most of the cute ’uns do that.
Then they send a chum to apply for the vacancy,
and he gets his little turn. Why, we’d have
to burst ourselves all round the town all day long,
if we worked at the business. But some of the
palaver part of the genuine cases is rare fun. We
dry the widow’s tears'and rescue the orphan fiom
want and woe, and provide for the grandfather in
his final teething, and comfort even the ancient
spinster grand-aunt, and patch the breeches of the
ragged and torn boy, and dower the maiden all
forlorn.”
Bob signed, of course, and the Universal
Mutual Benefit and Life Assurance Co., Unlimited,
issued another policy.
# # #
The above is based on an incident that
occurred within my personal observation about
18 months ago in an Australian city. I have
been told that the game is being liberally prac-
tised in other places that shall also be nameless.
It seems a pity to spoil it. How much the insur-
ance societies profit by it is a matter beyond my
arithmetical prowess.-
DIPSO.

The Wild-Cat Column.
So sensitive is the mining market that when a
year ago the sale of 5000 Broken Hill Proprietary shares
in one day would not affect it, a few days ago a holder of
•500 in a forced sale preferred to accept privately a price
£l below the ruling rates for the parcel rather than take his
chalices upon the market. Even 100 shares in any of the
principal Broken Hill mines, placed suddenly on the
Sydney market, would stagger it.
* # «
If the Victory mine, Charters Towers, were in
N.S.W., depressed as the mining market is the shares would
he double their present value. We do not thereby say
that they are too low, but a dividend gold mine in N.S.W.
is such a rara avis that the market can never measure it.
At Charters Towers, Bendigo, and other mining fields,
where there are local markets and shareholders, specula-
tion in mining shares—except in times of boom, when
everybody goes mad —is reduced to a system. Buyers
want to see at least 25 per cent, for their money, with
years of work in sight. If the latter is impossible because
■of the circumscribed character of the workings, then
■dividends must be at the rate of 50 up to 100 per cent.
The holders of stock who regulate the market are mining
men who act from their own observation, and not from
reports. In N.S.W. the declaration of a dividend is
regarded as something that will continue with the same
regularity as those of gas-companies and big hanks.
* * *
The biggest piece of gold-mining work now
being done in N.S.W. is that of the Delegate Sluicing Co..
a few miles out of Bombala. Some years ago a local syn-
dicate attempted to bring water on this ground by
constructing a race some twenty miles out, and, at the
finish, found it was too low to work the ground, which
was practically abandoned. It was last year brought
under the not ice of Mr. J. S. Reid, at one time the biggert
shareholder on the register of the Broken Hill Proprie-
tary. He had the ground carefully examined, and finally
determined to form a powerful company—an easy matter
for him at any time—to sluice away the ground with
water lifted from the river which runs some 250 feet
below. For this reason the Austral-OtisEngineering Co.,
of Melbourne, have turned out the biggest pumping-plant
known in Australia, capable of lifting 4£ million gallons a
day. It is now on its way to the mine. The estimated
expenditure before work is started is £36,000. Many of
the local inhabitants fear the result—apparently in ignor-
ance of the fact that three or four grains to the cubic yard
should pay, while half-a-pennyweight will make it a big
thing. The men who have initiated this work have the
refusal of nearly everything new in the Australian mining
world, and it is very unlikely that they would tackle a
rank duffer with £36,000, especially when its value can be
so easily ascertained. t~.-j
* * *
John Wallace —than whom few men in Victoria
have spent more in legitimate mining work—with a few
others is laying out some £26,000 in the building of a
dam,'erection of pumping-plant, and the construction of
a race for hydraulic sluicing purposes on the Shoalhaven
River. For many years the mining-warden of the district
has urged the N.S. W. Government to carry out a work
which is now being tackled by the enterprise of a few
Victdrians.
# # »
The Last Call was a magnificently-worked piece
of villainy. In purchasing the mine from the finder the
sellers to the Sydney syndicate were mistaken in their
judgment. They bought it for £BOO and had in sight a
rich ironstone vein or deposit. It subsequently dis-
appeared, but undoubtedly its gold was used by some
person or persons to deceive Hammond. The rule was
first run oxer the Mining Warden, who reported that his
prospects averaged over twelve ounces to the ton, and
generally described the property as a mining wonder.
While Hammond was on the road, telegrams from Clon-
curry, detailing the increasing richness of the property,
poured into Sydney, so much so that before Hammond
reached his destination he had offers to purchase, at £l,
shares of which he had the refusal of 80,000 at 2s. (id.
Naturally, he concluded he was in for a very big thing.
The vendors were very independent, some of them at any
rate genuinely so, for he it remembered that if the mine
was salted by any one of them, there were snm ■ at lca«t
who had not the slightest knowledge of it. It was here
that the veil was drawn over Hammond’s s isp'cious
nature, in a word, he must have regarded those he met
there as all honest men, or he would never have been so
deceived. He can take this as a compliment or other-
wise. The writer can tell him, as he could tell the writer,
if he would, how that deception occurred, but he dare
not. Attempting to gaol a sailer is an easy way of putting
yourself there.
* * *
Recollect that the best of mining men will make
mistakes. It was the proud boast of Mark Hammond,
ever since he made a fortune out of Beyers anti Holter-
mann’s, that, while he would by over-caution miss many
a good mine, he would never put himself or his friends
into a bad one.
# * *
The world judges by averages as well as by
results. The mining expert—we detest the designation—
who condemned every mine of, say, the last hundred
floated in N.S.W., would have such a magnificent average
as to entitle him to the leading position amongst
examiners.
# # •
Prices of mining stock are not regulated by the
value of the mine so much as by the inclination and
weaknesses of the holders, simply because nine-tenths of
them cannot even approximate the value. It is the
tenacity with which the novices hold on and the bold
ad\ertisement which they give to their ideas of value that
create the fictitious value of shares.
* # #
The Crown of the Peak announces the return of
some IGoz. from 115 tons of “seconds.” Why not “ forty-
seconds?” Why this everlasting excuse of seconds? It
is less than 3dwts. to the ton, anyhow.
* # *
It is said that Professor Sobnaebel’s report,
which cost the Broken Hill Proprietary some thousands,
is, in few respects, dissimilar from that given, after a
series of experiments of the Peelwood sulphide ores, some
two years ago at a cost of some five hundred.
* * *
The manager of a Southern Co. reports that
the “ whip is completed and works well ” —that’s all the
shareholders want to know. It is a matter of perfect in-
difference as to whether the shoot of gold (a narrow one
to all 1 appearances) is richer or poorer. Everything is
booming when it is proclaimed to the world in all the
newspapers that a log of wood with a little wheel at one
end, the whole of which cost 305., is working well.
# # #
How have the mighty fallen ! A short year
ago it was a compliment to get a seat on the Sydney
Stock Exchange, even if you planked £lOOO. Now less
than £3OO will buy one.
Messrs. Cleaver the well-known manu-
facturers of Juvenia Soap, announce a grand prize
competition in connection with their snap, full
particulars of which can bo obtained from the
agents, Feldheim, Gotthelf & Co., Sydney. The
fir.-t prize is a cash one of £3O, the second one
£lO, and then six prizes ranging down from £5 to
ss. Every purchaser of a tablet of their soap gets
a ticket entitling the holder to compete for the
prizes. This is a novelty and no doubt many of
our readers will avail themselves of the
opportunity.

Rennie affair. The Australian public never actually
lynches a man; but the Australian press does....

As, clambering down the background.
The villain breathes of death.
He nears the sleeping hero
And vents a fearful roar.
And then tucks up his shirt-sleeve
And hares his blade for gore.
Meanwhile, the virtuous person.
Still in the limelight sheen,
Springs up and draws his bowie.
Illuminates the scene.
The weapons clang and clatter,
The sparks fly thick and fast,
They fight all round the precipice,
Spectators stand aghast.
Ila, ha, the villain’s weapon,
Falls flashing on the stage,
And headlong down the canyon
He dives, in battled rage.
A paper flutters from him,
While hanging in suspense,
Tiie victor reads: “ What, this ! It is !
It proves my innocence.”
These are the good old fictions
That come from Surrey Side —
And prosperous they are who
Such pabulum provide.
QUANDONG.
Forty-five per cent of the world’s wool is
produced by Australia.
The bulk of the London property is in the
hands of 5000 people, and you can narrow these
down to 500, 50, and 5, as the landlordry increases,
geometrically.
One, Beaton, writes to a Charters Towers
IN. Q.) paper, showing that .£3BO ss. was the
profit made on 25 acres of sugarcane,or £l5 2s. per
acre. .lamps Tracksou recently stated in the
Queenslander that ho got from GO to 80 tons of
sugar per acre, yielding him from £3O to £4O
profit per acre. Last year, the total output of '
sugar in Bananaland was 00,0831, tons, .or more
than 10,000 tons over the output of ’OO. The
argument that the sugar industry is dying because
it cannot use Kanaka labour is a wilful deception ;
the planters are merely gluttonous for still greater
profits.
Murrieta and Co., the great London
bankers, have burst, and the bailiffs are in
possession. When Barings collapsed the Bank of
England found the money to avert niter insol-.
vency on the security of certain guarantors, of
whom Murrieta was one. If it hadn’t done so
there would have been a smash which would bavo
wrecked half the financial institutions in London,
the Bank of England, most probably, among the
rest. In other words, the Bank propped up
Baring, and Murrieta propped up the Bank, and
now there is nobody to prop up Murrieta, and
the time is getting near when all the financial
daddocks will go together. At latest advices
Barings owed the Bank of England £6,500,000,
and the only security for it was the guarantee of
Murrieta and a few other institutions, allot' which
were also busy guaranteeing each other astheonly
means of keeping afloat. Which, taking it all
round, is much the same as Bell and Davis guar-
anteeing Matthias Larkin, and Larkin guarantee-
ing Bell and Davis, and Parkes giving his
promissory-note as security for the lot, and the
p.n. being duly endorsed by the Northumberland
Banking Co.
The United Kingdom has been a woeful
place ever since Clarence died. Great, grief-
stricken, befogged, and murky Loudon is swamped
in crape, and every human-being belonging to
“fashionable society” looks like a moving ink-
blot against a background of wretchedness. The
dress circle at the leg-show is black with dreary
mourning. All the great shabby-genteel multi-
tude from one end of the kingdom -to-the other has
put on tiie rusty garb of woe in.order-to create a
baleful impression that it moved habitually in
royal society, and that the death of Clarence is to
It as the loss of a near friend. The haggard widow,
vvith mittens and corkscrew ringlets, who keeps a
boarding-house, sits up with a pained expression
of countenance on the black, slippery, horsehair-
iofa in the parlour. The grocery-woman and
the cheesemongering female are got up in black
jostumes—as a rule the costumes are recently
dyed and still damp, and when the grief-stricken
person gets too near the fire a cloud of steam
rises from her as if she were a bereaved relative
hying on a clothes-line. Even the infant, in
middle-class society, is expected to look sad and
pre-oocupied and to wear a far-away expression.
Banquets and public feeds have been stopped, and
the melancholy waiter is going into the work-
house. The hansom-cabmen are fast going
lead-broke; people avoid the hansom as
much as possible in times of national
lamentation; the solemn, cheap, and infected
four-wheeler is held to bo better adapted to
the situation. Most of the drapers are approach-
ing ruin ; people under ordinary conditions habitu-
dly buy new clothes, but they don t buy new
mourning-the age and rustiness of the latter is
an additional evidence of the wearer’s woe. Even
window curtains aren't washed so regularly as
they used to be, and front steps are allowed to get
murky as an evidence that the inhabitant inside
has forgotten them owing to the great trouble that
is preying on his vitals. Everybody is wearing
out his old, disused bell-topper—the crape-band
serving to conceal the bash in the brim ; every
paper is still talking about the “ illustrious dead
it is raining most of the time and the streets are
sodden, and a few enthusiasts have still got a half-
mast flag flopping wretchedly in the breeze—and
yet nobody, or hardly anybody, really cares
whether Clarence is dead or not. The nation is
only weeping through force of habit and inherited
grovel.
