The Rev. Henry Martyn’s Saved Souls.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Some Northern stories are good, but not
true. Others are true, but not good. Others are
not true and not good either. This story is both
good and true.
# * #
Ten years ago the Rev. Henry Martyn
was Church of England minister at Sheet-o’-bark,
N.Q. Sheet-o’-bark was then a wilder but a
busier place than it is now. Two big sugar-
plantations were in full swing just outside the
township. Both have since been ruined by bad
management; but in the Rev. Henry Martyn’s time
there were nearly 400 South Sea islanders on the
two plantations ; with over a hundred women, and
children too numerous to count.
It was the keenest pleasure the parson had to
ride out and baptise according to the rites of the
Church of England the babies that cropped up
irregularly every month. “ Brands plucked from
the burning,” he called them. The dusky fathers
and mothers did not object but rather approved
of the ceremony as helping to drive away the
debil-debil and providing some safe-guard against
measles.
But there was a very large fly in the Rev.
Henry’s ointment. The Sheet-o’-bark R.C. priest,
Father Murphy by name, had also con-
ceived a vehement desire to rescue the perishing
Kanaka, and very often when the parson reached
the plantations and had washed off the dirt of his
20-mile ride, he found that his rival had been
before him, and had christened every available
infant after the Romish fashion, besides establish-
ing a lien on the souls of several who were yet
in embryo, by dexterously cajoling the prospec-
tive mammas.
It appeared to be all the same to the Kanakas
which faith their progeny professed; but once the
creed was chosen they stuck to it. Round_ the
neck of every child that Father Murphy baptised,
he hung a cheap rosary—“ the mark of the beast”
the Rev. Henry privately called it - and no per-
suas:on or solicitation could prevail upon the
parents to take it off. The parson found some
consolation in the fact that the priest was not per-
mitted to meddle with the little flock whom he
sprinkled, but it out him to the heart to see the
number in the opposition fold.
By degi’ees the competition grew keener. Priest
and parson had been accustomed to make one
visit a week to the plantations. The one became
two, and the two three. At last the jealousy
grew so great that each paid a scout to ride in
and inform him when a birth was expected ; and
the plantation doctor had to make a rule that no
baby should be baptised under twenty-four hours
of age, the inconsiderate zeal of the Rev. Henry
having watered one very young youngster so
liberally that it expired in convulsions ten
minutes afterwards. Still, its soul was safe; and,
as the parson remarked, that was the great point.
# # *
The Rev. Henry Martyn was riding along
the road to the Yankalilla plantation, one
November morning ten years ago. He was not a
bad-looking man—careless of appearance, but with
a winning face that made friends everywhere. His
horse was jaded; for he had ridden to Yankalilla
and back on the preceding day without being
rewarded by a solitary christening. But the first
thing that morning his scout had arrived, panting
with the news that there had been two births at
Bindoola during the night.
Two souls to save ! The Rev. Henry was used
to the saddle, though he felt rather stiff. His
horse looked stiffer, but the good man never
hesitated for an instant. He took a hurried
breakfast and started at once. Father Murphy
had had all the luck lately, and the harvest of
small Catholics was plentiful. All the more
reason for the Rev. Henry to make haste, and
though a merciful man to his beast, he stuck the
spurs in vigorously. But hardly had he got two
miles out of town when he met Father Murphy’s
scout, whom he knew by sight, and hated as
much as bis gentle nature permitted, cantering
quickly along, with his news plainly written on
his face.
The Rev. Henry made a quick calculation. It
was a quarter-past nine. The road was a first-
class one—the best in Queensland —level and well-
made, but it was a good eighteen miles to
Bindoola. His horse was obviously flagging. At
best he could not reach the plantation under two
hours. Two hours ! In a quarter of an hour at
most Father Murphy, whose horseflesh was as
good as his whisky, would have the news; and
the Rev. Henry knew him too well not to be sure
that in ten minutes more he would be cantering
along the Bindoola track. His scout would be .
bound to tell him who was in front, and the
information would doubtless have its effect.
The parson reckoned, in the first place, that he
had forty minutes start; in the second place, that
the priest, going twice as fast, would catch up to
him in an hour and twenty minutes; thirdly, that
Father Murphy would reach Bindoola at least
twenty minutes in front of him; and in twenty
minutes at a push one could baptize every blessed
Kanaka on the plantation. His mind was made
up. He would press on to Tinwood’s selection,
over the river, borrow a fresh horse and—two
souls would be saved. His spurs were red now.
“ Man proposes,” and then the other fellow
interferes and upsets the proposition. The Rev.
Henry had gone some six miles further, at the
best speed he could muster, when his horse stag-
gered and stopped dead lame. Here was a quan-
dary. He was just in sight of the Roberts River,
Tinwood’s was two miles farther on, Father
Murphy was 20 minutes behind coming up
steadily at a hand-gallop and two souls to save.
He had nlechanically walked down to the bridge
leading his limping horse. The Roberts was a
strong stream at any time; but there had been
heavy rains on its watershed during the last few
days, and the water was nearly up to the bridge.
He led the horse across. If the bridge was
washed away now .
Just then his eye caught the glitter of steel by
the side of the road. The Divisional Board
labourers had been making some repairs to the
bridge and had gone on further, and the tools
they did not need were left till they returned.
There were axes, saws, and a stout crowbar.
In a flash a tempting demon had possession of
the Rev. Henry.
# # #
The bridge was some 40ft. long. Piles
had been driven in the middle of the stream, and
each span was bridged by two tough, hardwood
girders a foot in diameter. On these lay the
decking with a slight railing on each side. The
bridge had only been up five years ; but the wet
seasons and floods had played havoc with it and
heavy traffic had to cross by the ford some hun-
dred yards below. The Rev. Henry seized a
crowbar with the uncertain motions of a preacher
in a dream. He had little trouble in prizing off
the decking, including some new boards which
had been put on by the works party the previous
day. He worked with the furious energy of a
madman. He remembered afterwards reflecting
that someone might come along the scrub track at
any moment and surprise him ; but the idea left
no impression at the time.
Steadily working, in five minutes he had tossed
all the decking up to the middle of the bridge into
the river. Not an instant was wasted. Father
Murphy would be up in ten minutes. He moved
as coolly and methodically as a somnambulist.
Stepping back along the girder, he seized a two-
handled saw, and began to use it with desperate
strength. Had the girder merely rested on the
bank, he would have tried to prize it off; but it
ran eight or ten feet into an abutment of loose
stones. The saw was his only resource. For-
tunately, it would only be necessary to cut through
one girder. A bold man might get a horse across
on two with the aid of some saplings; but he
could do nothing with one.
A foot of hardwood to saw through in five
minutes !—for Father Murphy might come up at
any moment, and the Rev. Henry needed two
minutes to get under cover. Luckily for him, the
sun had split the girder, the ants had bored it,
and the water had rotted it. His heart palpitated
. with the unaccustomed labour; but m four
minutes he had the satisfaction of seeing the
girder drop into the stream. The other end
loosened by the shock, held out an instant against
the force of the rushing water ; then it too fell,
and the heavy log was whirled round and round
by the current in its progress to the sea.
Of the Roberts Bridge which cost the Divisional
Board <£3oo, half remained intact. The other
half was represented by a solitary girder. And
the Rev. Henry Martyn, M.A., clerk in holy
orders, minister of the Established Church of
England, and pastor of the Sheet-o’-bark congre-
gation, had done the deed.
He threw down the saw, and hurriedly led his
lame steed round the turning of the road. He
was not a moment too soon. As he turned the
corner he heard the regular footfall of a cantering
horse coming up to the bridge behind him. Father
Murphy had gained five minutes on his calcula-
tions.
The good father looked in amazement at the
ruins of the bridge. Peering over to the other
side he could see the sawn end of the girder. But
he could form no theory to account for an event
so unaccountable. He plainly saw that he could
not cross the bridge to Bindoola, and that his rival
had crossed but one glance at the boiling water
proved sufficiently. “ One might get to heaven
that way,” he muttered, “but not to Bindoola.”
He waited an hour, but no one came to the other
side of the bridge though he was joined on the
town side by three horsemen. One of them crept
along the remaining girder and gave it as his
opinion that there had been foul play. Another
tried the ford, and was nearly drowned without
getting across. The third, asking the priest to
take his horse back, made his way along the
girder and walked the rest of the journey.
Father Murphy sighed deeply. The light was
gone out of his day. Then leading the horse
entrusted to him he rode back to town to tell
the news.
# * *
The Rev. Henry Martyn walked on to
Tinwood’s. He was prostrated by the severe and
unusual labor he had performed ; his clothes were
wringing wet; and he looked as unclerical as any
scarecrow. But his physical anguish was nothing
to his anguish of mind. All the possible conse-
quences of his act now rose threateningly before
his shuddering consciousness. He saw the wide-
eyed astonishment of his congregation, of his
friends, of his bishop, when they learned his
guilt; he pictured himself in a felon’s cell supping
hominy and picking oakum; and to all the horrid
pictures which his lively fancy conjured up his
tender conscience played rythmical accompani-
ment, the burden of which was that the detection
of his sin was a small matter compared with the
gravity of that sin itself. He wished he had
never seen Bindoola, never come to Sheet-o’-bark,
never joined the ministry, never been born.
When he got to Tinwood’s he accounted for his
appearance by his long walk in the sun. The
horse was readily lent him, and when he had
taken a glass of milk and rested himself, he felt
refreshed and better able to look calmly at his
exploit. Before he had reached Yankalilla he
had made up his mind that confession was im-
possible.
His spirits rose, too, when he found things at
the plantation were even brighter than they were
represented. He baptised three infants, and as
he rode away next morning his spirits rose. At
Tinwood’s he found his own horse sufficiently
rested to carry him home slowly, and learnt the
great news that the Roberts Bridge had been cut
by some villains unknown.
“ What time did you cross, Mr. Martyn ? ”
queried Tinwood.
“I think it would be about 10 o’clock,” be
replied in a steady voice.
“ And Johnson came down at half-past ten and
found half of it gone,” commented Tinwood. “ Xt’s
a mvstery to me how they did it.”
“Do you think I’ll be able to get across’”
asked the Rev. Henry.
“ Oh, I think so ! ” said Tinwood. “ The road-
gang were at it all yesterday afternoon. The
cheek of the fellows in taking their tools amuses
me. ” .
The Rev. Henry rode down to the river. There
some curious residents were watching the opera-
tions of the road-party and discussing the wonder-
ful event. The workmen had got a new girder in
place and were laying a temporary decking.
“ Good day, sir,” said the Board’s overseer,
who knew the parson well, “this is a nice job,
this here.”
“ What could have been the object of the
persons who cut it ! ” asked the guilty parson
with dry lips.
“Blest if I know, sir; it seems to me nothing
but pure mischief.”
In such conversation the Rev. Henry was
compelled to take part until the bridge was
sufficiently repaired for the horse to cross over;
when he rode away bursting with shame at his
horrible hypocrisy.
* * *
There was a magisterial enquiry, at which
the Rev. Henry Martyn gave evidence as to the
circumstances connected with the cutting of the
Roberts Bridge; but nothing material was
elicited. Perhaps Father Murphy smelt a rat;
but if he did—well, he was a gentleman and held
his tongue. Peace to his ashes ! Malarial fever
carried him off half a generation ago.
The Divisional Board offered a reward of <£so
for the conviction of the offenders; the police
arrested several unlucky waifs and strays and dis-
charged them again, and Martyn secretly gave
them a donation apiece to ease his conscience ; but
the mystery has never been explained till now. If
there is any moral to this story, it is that when a
parson has done a shady action he should lie low
like any ordinary sinner and say nothing about it.

A STORY OF SPINIFEX LAND.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“ Can’t find ’em, can’t you ?”
The speaker—a tall, middle-aged man—was
leaning against the tail of a horse-dray, addressing
a trembling black boy, of about 14.
“ Now, just you be off again, and if you are not
back with the whole lot before sunset down there,”
and the speaker indicated a certain point in the
heavens, “I’ll hide the immortal soul out of you.
I heard the bells just before daylight, so they
must be camped close handy.”
The boy gave a_ longing look at the fire, for the
winter mornings in North Queensland are sharp,
and he had nothing on but his shirt; then, with
the bridle trailing over his arm, he departed in
search of the missing horses.
His master, owner, employer (call him what
you will) lit his pipe and laid himself down upon
his blankets for a smoke. He was a man who
prided himself on having been a gentleman and
owning an aristocratic name. He also boasted
that he could break in a black boy properly (a
small boy, of course), and had a general,
all-round swagger about him that, however,
could always be reduced by reference to
a certain of a station he was once
employed on. What time he was conspicuous
by his sudden disappearance and immediate
return, after the trouble was over, with the
announcement that he had gone to the back-
verandah of the kitchen to get his revolver. Some
of the boys said they found his tracks under the
water-tank.
The sun was high above the spot indicated
before the boy returned, mounted, barebacked, on
an old horse with a muffled bell round its neck.
He dropped wearily from its back, having been
belting it along with the hobbles and his bare
heels, fora couple of miles or more. “’N other
fellow gone,” he said regarding the “ white ’ man
timidly, “This one bell you bin hear.”
. Delcourt, the individual in question, got up
silently and went to the tail of the dray. “ Last
time I send you after horse,” he muttered. “ Put
my saddle on,” he continued, “I’ll go myself.”
The boy saddled the old horse obediently while
Delcourt, after rummaging for some time, finally
came up with a pair of very small handcuffs.
The boy guessed, from experience, what was
coining; but, after all, it was no great hardship
to be handcuffed to the dray-wheel until Delcourt
came back. He could sleep, and he was tired.
Naturally he supposed that was all that was in-
tended—to prevent him running away. He had
been served so before.
Delcourt told the boy to go under the dray and
he down. He did so, and his hands were hand-
cuffed through the spokes of the wheel, over his
head, as he lay on his back. Then his “em-
ployer ’ fastened his shins to two spokes of the
opposite wheel with a couple of saddle-straps.
This the boy did not quite understand, but he was
helpless, his feet sticking out from beneath the
dray as far as his ankles, or a little above.
“Now, my lad, I’ll make you hop along quick
next tune you go to look for horses for me. I’ll
roast your lazy toes for you. You’ve been sitting
under a tree all the morning with the old horse’s
bell tied up. FU teach you.”
. He dragged a burning log from the fire and put
it close to the soles of the boy’s feet, then he piled
on a few more sticks, and, getting on the horse
rode away.
For the first few minutes while the fire still
smouldered the warmth was not unpleasant, and
the wearied lad dozed off. He was soon awakened.
The fire blazed up brightly, and agonising yells
began to resound through the solitude. They
brought no answer, save the caws of a frightened
crow, disturbed from its meal at the deserted
camp-fire. In about four hours’ time Delcourt
returned with the missing horses. The fire had
burned itself out, and the boy was either senseless
or dead.
He released him, dragged him from under the
dray, and threw a blanket over him, for at that
moment he heard voices and the tread of horses
coming from another direction.
He was busy hobbling the horses when the new-
comers arrived on the scene. Two men with a
couple of packhorses.
“ W hy, Delcourt,” said the first one to dis-
mount, a slight, dark, wiry little man with a
crippled hand, “you’ve not got far; we did not
expect to overtake you for another thirty miles.”
“I’ve been after my horses all day, ” returned
Delcourt, rising from his task, “and my bov is
sick too.”
“ What’s the matter with him ?” said the other,
a man with white beard and hair.
“ Fever, 1 suppose, you know how blacks cave
in.”
One of the men stopped over the boy and
turned the blanket back. “He looks d d
queer,” he said; “give me some water.” The
other handed him a quart-pot full, and he bathed
the boy’s face and breast, until he revived some-
what, and drank greedily of the remainder.
“ You fellows going to turn out here?” said
Delcourt coolly at this juncture. Neither answered.
The boy, in his clutch at the quart-pot, threw
aside the blanket, and the keen eyes of the shorter
man were attracted by the appearance of the boy’s
feet.
‘‘What’s the meaning of this, Delcourt?” he
said sharply.
“O,” returned the other carelessly, “ we came
over some fresh burnt country yesterday, and
the young beggar had been hammering his horse
so I made him walk.”
“You liar!” returned the other fiercely.
“We’ve seen your tracks for nearly sixty miles,
and there is not an acre of burnt country between
here and the foot of the tableland. Besi les I’ve
seen scores and hundreds of niggers hunting over
freshly burnt country, but never saw one with
feet like that;” and he pointed to the scorched
and crippled members.
The elder man had arisen by this time, and now
spoke before Delcourt could collect himself.
“ This is some of your work,” he said, “ I have
heard of it before. This time by (here
he swore an oath rather too common in the
spinifex country) yon shall answer for it.”
“The boy did it himself,” said Delcourt sul-
lenly in answer, without resenting the lammase
used to him.
“ That shall be seen to,” said the other. “ Re-
member both I and T here are magistrates,
* 8 n °t brought safely into
i il nave a warrant out against you for murder.”
Ihe boy never came in. Delconrt said that he
ran away ! Nobody could do anything. Nobody
could prove he didn’t run away, although every-
body believed it to be a lie. Delcourt got a worse
name than before, that was all, and he didn’t
mind that.
Retributive justice overtook him many years
& i er^ ar^S ,n an °*ber land, where the natives
slew him. But although in a certain sense it was
retributive it was not poetical justice; for a
British man-o’-war came along and shot the
natives for wiping him out.

An amiable crank in Sydney D.T. suggests
that legislation be immediately introduced to put
a stop to street-smoking. What! Before full
local option has become the law of the land?
Never ! It is too previous.

That convenient “correspondent” who
always writes to Sydney Echo to say the things
that the Echo is afraid to say itself, turns up once
more, as follows :
“ Curious ” writes:—As an instance of the superiority
of articles sold in freetrade countries as compared with
those of the same kind sold under a protective policy it
may be pointed out that the cement used by Deeming at
Rainhill in freetrade England completely prevented the
escape of effluvia from the bodies buried under the
hearthstone. The cement used in Melbourne did not, it
would seem, hermetically seal the hole under the hearth-
stone at Windsor.
By a strange coincidence an equally genuine cor-
respondent has just written to The Bulletin
pointing out the great superiority of the Aus-
tralian protected policeman over the British free-
trade constable. The poor, miserable officer who
is raised on Foreign-trade principles never even
knew that wholesale murder has been going on,
while the “ cop ” who is grown upon a sound
Protectionist system caught the assassin right off.

Donald (Vic.) Express on the 17th of
Ireland :
St. Patrick’s Day broke early, and it broke smiling.
Just glance at the smiling faces on all sides. See how the
very atmosphere is radiated by the brightness of the
happy countenances, and responds in joyous vibrations to
the glowing feelings of the laughing multitude. See how
the dazzling eyes and beaming cheeks of the beauteous
maidens are reflected in the sunlit heavens, and the gold
of the sun dancing on the glinting brow of the occasion is
sent glistening back again like flrerays falling upon a
lake of diamonds. Diamonds ! Ah ! And what priceless
diamonds jewels, my bhoy—of human beauty sparkled
and glittered on all sides.
It reads like a quotation from Dan. O’Connor.
“WHY HE NEVER LOOKED BEHIND
HIM ”
“ After this I never looked behind me.”
This is a very common expression. What do
people mean by it ? Lot’s wife looked behind her
and was changed into a pillar of salt. A locomo-
tive driver in America looked behind him one day
last summer and so didn’t see an open drawback
in front of him. Hence a wreck and great loss of
life. A man in London faded to look behind him
and was run down by a hansom. What shall we
do as a rule? Look behind us or not ?
We introduce a man who says he never looked
behind him —after a certain time. How are we to
take his meaning ? Why, by letting him explain
it.
He goes on to say that in one day in February,
1890, he was suddenly seized with dizziness and
pain in his head. Like all healthy people, under
similar circumstances, he didn’t know what to
make of it. He says he felt strange and queer, he
shivered as though the weather had suddenly
turned cold, and then flushed witli the heat as
though it had turned hot again. What ailed him?
His doctor said he was attacked with influenza,
and ordered him to bed. He went to bed. A few
days later the fever left him, but the illness did
not. It merely assumed another form. His
tongue looked like a piece of brown leather, and
his skin and the whites of his eyes became yellow,
like old parchment. We must all eat to live, but
when this man tried to eat, the food went against
him, and after he had swallowed it by main force,
it caused such pain in the chest, side, and stomach
that he wished he had let it alone. Then his
heart began to palpitate, and he says he felt low,
languid, and tired. He had what he calls a sink-
ing feeling at the pit of the stomach and a
craving which nothing satisfied.
Being unable to take any but liquid food he
grew so weak that he was barely able to walk.
Then his heart troubled him once more, and, to
quote his own words, “As I sat in my chair I
could hear my heart thumping as if somebody teas
pounding me on the hack. ”
This showed that the heart had too much work
on hand and was struggling under it like a horse
trying to carry two men. “ I got very little sleep
at night,” he says, “and would lie awake for
hours tossing about on the bed.” This sort of
thing is very wearing, and we are not surprised to
learn that he lost flesh until little was left of him
but skin and bone. “My cheeks,” he says, “ sank
in until they were almost drawn together, and
people shook their heads and predicted that my
time in this world was nearly up. Still I had all
confidence in my physician and kept on taking his
medicine. From first to last I took some forty or
fifty bottles of it (of all kinds) without benefit.
“Finally one day the doctor sounded my lungs
and asked me if any of our family died of con-
sumption. He said that the heart palpitation was
caused by dyspepsia. Then he said I had better
take further advice; he could do no more for me.
This was after nine months of his treatment. I
gave up all hopes of getting better, and, indeed,
no one expected me to.
“It was now winter again, December, 1890.
One day I found a little book or pamphlet in the
house, that I had never seen before. It was about
a medicine called Mother Seigel’s Syrup, arid
described a case like mine having been cured by it.
Without going into all my hopes and fears on the
point, it is enough to say that I got a bottle from
Mr. Kirkman, Chemist, Ellerby Lane. I took the
contents of that bottle and certainly felt a little
better. I took a second and began to eat solid
food, which agreed with me.
“ After this I never looked behind me, though
my recovery was a work of time, for I was very
much reduced. I stuck to the medicine, and with
good reason, and at last got back to my work,
strong and well, and have remained so ever since.'
When I went back to the works the foreman and
others gathered round me and asked what had
wrought the wonderful change. I answered,
“ Mother Seigel’s Syrup had wrought it.” When
I said I wished to start work they told me I must
first be examined by a doctor. The doctor said I
was fit for work and I went to work the next
morning and have never lost a minute since.
“ I wish others to know what Seigel’s Syrup has
done for me, and I give the proprietors permission
to publish this brief account of my case. lam a
cloth presser by trade and have worked at Messrs.
Hepworth and Sous, Clay Pit Lane, for four
years.” Harvey Askew, 2 Back Timber Place,
Ellerby Lane, Leeds.
The doctor was right in saying that the apparent
heart trouble in Mr. Askew's case arose from
dyspepsia, for dyspepsia was his only ailment.
And if he had used Mother Seigel’s Syrup in
February, 1890, he would have had no tale to tell,
for he would have been all right directly. As it
is we are glad that after he did try it lie had no
relapse. He never looked behind him.
